Cochrane Database of Systematic reviews Review - Intervention

Sucrose analgesia for heel‐lance procedures in neonates

  • Janet Yamada
  • Mariana Bueno
  • Lucia Santos
  • Sarah Haliburton
  • Marsha Campbell-Yeo
  • Bonnie Stevens
Authors' declarations of interest

Version published: 30 August 2023 Version history

https://doi.org/10.1002/14651858.CD014806
Collapse all Expand all

Abstract

available in
  • English
  • Español
  • فارسی
  • Français
  • ภาษาไทย
  • 简体中文

    • Background

    Sucrose has been examined for calming and pain‐relieving effects in neonates for invasive procedures such as heel lance.

    Objectives

    To assess the effectiveness of sucrose for relieving pain from heel lance in neonates in terms of immediate and long‐term outcomes

    Search methods

    We searched (February 2022): CENTRAL, MEDLINE, Embase, PsycINFO, CINAHL, Web of Science, and three trial registries.

    Selection criteria

    We included randomised controlled trials where term and/or preterm neonates received sucrose for heel lances. Comparison treatments included water/placebo/no intervention, non‐nutritive sucking (NNS), glucose, breastfeeding, breast milk, music, acupuncture, facilitated tucking, and skin‐to‐skin care.

    Data collection and analysis

    We used standard Cochrane methods. We reported mean differences (MD) with 95% confidence intervals (CI) using the fixed‐effect model for continuous outcome measures. We assessed heterogeneity by the I2 test. We used GRADE to assess certainty of evidence.

    Main results

    We included 55 trials (6273 infants): 29 included term neonates, 22 included preterm neonates, and four included both. Heel lance was investigated in 50 trials; 15 investigated other minor painful procedures in addition to lancing.

    Sucrose vs control

    The evidence suggests that sucrose probably results in a reduction in PIPP scores compared to the control group at 30 seconds (MD ‐1.74 (95% CI ‐2.11 to ‐1.37); I2 = 62%; moderate‐certainty evidence) and 60 seconds after lancing (MD ‐2.14, 95% CI ‐3.34 to ‐0.94; I2 = 0%; moderate‐certainty evidence).

    The evidence is very uncertain about the effects of sucrose on DAN scores compared to water at 30 seconds after lancing (MD ‐1.90, 95% CI ‐8.58 to 4.78; heterogeneity not applicable (N/A); very low‐certainty evidence).

    The evidence suggests that sucrose probably results in a reduction in NIPS scores compared to water immediately after lancing (MD ‐2.00, 95% CI ‐2.42 to ‐1.58; heterogeneity N/A; moderate‐certainty evidence).

    Sucrose vs NNS

    The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on PIPP scores compared to NNS during the recovery period after lancing (MD 0.60, 95% CI ‐0.30 to 1.50; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence) and on DAN scores at 30 seconds after lancing (MD ‐1.20, 95% CI ‐7.87 to 5.47; heterogeneity N/A; very low‐certainty evidence).

    Sucrose + NNS vs NNS

    The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose + NNS on PIPP scores compared to NNS during lancing (MD ‐4.90, 95% CI ‐5.73 to ‐4.07; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence) and during recovery after lancing (MD ‐3.80, 95% CI ‐4.47 to ‐3.13; heterogeneity N/A; very low‐certainty evidence).

    The evidence is very uncertain about the effects of sucrose + NNS on NFCS scores compared to water + NNS during lancing (MD ‐0.60, 95% CI ‐1.47 to 0.27; heterogeneity N/A; very low‐certainty evidence).

    Sucrose vs glucose

    The evidence suggests that sucrose results in little to no difference in PIPP scores compared to glucose at 30 seconds (MD 0.26, 95% CI ‐0.70 to 1.22; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence) and 60 seconds after lancing (MD ‐0.02, 95% CI ‐0.79 to 0.75; heterogeneity N/A; low‐certainty evidence).

    Sucrose vs breastfeeding

    The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on PIPP scores compared to breastfeeding at 30 seconds after lancing (MD ‐0.70, 95% CI ‐0.49 to 1.88; I2 = 94%; very low‐certainty evidence).

    The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on COMFORTneo scores compared to breastfeeding after lancing (MD ‐2.60, 95% CI ‐3.06 to ‐2.14; heterogeneity N/A; very low‐certainty evidence).

    Sucrose vs expressed breast milk

    The evidence suggests that sucrose may result in little to no difference in PIPP‐R scores compared to expressed breast milk during (MD 0.3, 95% CI ‐0.24 to 0.84; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence) and at 30 seconds after lancing (MD 0.3, 95% CI ‐0.11 to 0.71; heterogeneity N/A; low‐certainty evidence).

    The evidence suggests that sucrose probably may result in slightly increased PIPP‐R scores compared to expressed breast milk 60 seconds after lancing (MD 1.10, 95% CI 0.34 to 1.86; heterogeneity N/A; low‐certainty evidence).

    The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on DAN scores compared to expressed breast milk 30 seconds after lancing (MD ‐1.80, 95% CI ‐8.47 to 4.87; heterogeneity N/A; very low‐certainty evidence).

    Sucrose vs laser acupuncture

    There was no difference in PIPP‐R scores between sucrose and music groups; however, data were reported as medians and IQRs.

    The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on NIPS scores compared to laser acupuncture during lancing (MD ‐0.86, 95% CI ‐1.43 to ‐0.29; heterogeneity N/A; very low‐certainty evidence).

    Sucrose vs facilitated tucking

    The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on total BPSN scores compared to facilitated tucking during lancing (MD ‐2.27, 95% CI ‐4.66 to 0.12; heterogeneity N/A; very low‐certainty evidence) and during recovery after lancing (MD ‐0.31, 95% CI ‐1.72 to 1.10; heterogeneity N/A; very low‐certainty evidence).

    Sucrose vs skin‐to‐skin + water (repeated lancing)

    The evidence suggests that sucrose results in little to no difference in PIPP scores compared to skin‐to‐skin + water at 30 seconds after 1st (MD 0.13, 95% CI ‐0.70 to 0.96); 2nd (MD ‐0.56, 95% CI ‐1.57 to 0.45); or 3rd lancing (MD‐0.15, 95% CI ‐1.26 to 0.96); heterogeneity N/A, low‐certainty evidence for all comparisons.

    The evidence suggests that sucrose results in little to no difference in PIPP scores compared to skin‐to‐skin + water at 60 seconds after 1st (MD –0.61, 95% CI ‐1.55 to 0.33); 2nd (MD ‐0.12, 95% CI ‐0.99 to 0.75); or 3rd lancing (MD‐0.40, 95% CI ‐1.48 to 0.68); heterogeneity N/A, low‐certainty evidence for all comparisons.

    Minor adverse events required no intervention.

    Authors' conclusions

    Sucrose compared to control probably results in a reduction of PIPP scores 30 and 60 seconds after single heel lances (moderate‐certainty evidence). Evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose compared to NNS, breastfeeding, laser acupuncture, facilitated tucking, and the effect of sucrose + NNS compared to NNS in reducing pain. Sucrose compared to glucose, expressed breast milk, and skin‐to‐skin care shows little to no difference in pain scores. Sucrose combined with other nonpharmacologic interventions should be used with caution, given the uncertainty of evidence.

    Plain language summary

    available in
  • English
  • Español
  • فارسی
  • Français
  • 日本語
  • Bahasa Malaysia
  • Polski
  • ภาษาไทย
  • 简体中文
  • 繁體中文

    • Sucrose for analgesia (pain relief) in newborn infants undergoing heel lance

    Key messages

    ‐ Sucrose, compared to control interventions, probably reduces pain for a single painful event (heel lance).

    ‐ There were a few minor immediate adverse events, such as gagging, that resolved without intervention.

    ‐ We need studies that assess the effect of repeated sucrose administration on immediate (pain intensity) and long‐term (neurodevelopmental) outcomes.

    ‐ We need to assess how sucrose affects neonates who are extremely preterm, unstable, or ventilated (or a combination of these factors).

    What is sucrose analgesia?

    Sucrose (table sugar) is mixed with water in various concentrations (usually 24%) and is given to babies in very small amounts (e.g. a few drops) about two minutes before a painful procedure. Sucrose is also given with other nondrug pain‐relieving interventions such as a pacifier (non‐nutritive sucking ‐ NNS) or when the baby is receiving skin‐to‐skin care.

    Why is this important for newborn babies who are undergoing heel lance procedures?

    Babies who are sick or immature, and require a stay in hospital, have several painful procedures per day and multiple painful procedures throughout the hospital stay; for most babies, the majority of painful procedures are heel lances. If untreated, pain has immediate and long‐term consequences that can impact their brain and behavioural development. It is important to minimise the number of painful procedures and the associated pain and suffering.

    What did we want to find out?

    We wanted to know how well sucrose worked to relieve pain in newborn babies who are having heel lance procedures while they were in hospital. We also wanted to know whether there were any safety concerns about using sucrose to relieve pain from single painful events such as heel lance.

    What did we do?

    We searched for studies that investigated:

    ‐ Sucrose compared to no treatment or standard care;

    ‐ Sucrose compared to water, other sweet solutions (e.g. glucose);

    ‐ Sucrose compared to another nondrug intervention (e.g. NNS);

    ‐ Whether sucrose was effective in decreasing the amount and severity of pain that babies experienced as assessed by baby’s pain responses (e.g. crying, grimacing, heart rate) and by using standardised infant pain scales.

    We also wanted to know if sucrose was associated with any unwanted effects in preterm or term babies.

    We compared and summarised the results of the studies and rated our confidence in the evidence, based on factors such as study methods.

    What did we find out?

    We reviewed 55 studies that included a total of 6273 babies. Twenty‐nine studies had full‐term babies only, 22 had preterm babies only, and four had both full‐term and preterm babies. Heel lance was the painful procedure in 50 studies. Five studies investigated a variety of other minor painful procedures in addition to heel lancing.

    Main results

    ‐ Sucrose compared to the control treatment probably reduces pain from single heel lances in babies.

    ‐ Results are uncertain about the effect of sucrose compared to NNS, breastfeeding, laser acupuncture, and facilitated tucking, in reducing pain scores.

    ‐ Results are uncertain about the effect of sucrose + NNS compared to NNS, in reducing pain.

    ‐ Sucrose does not seem to reduce pain from single heel lances compared to glucose, expressed breast milk, and skin‐to‐skin care.

    ‐ Reported adverse events were minor and resolved without any intervention.

    What are the limitations of the evidence?

    We have moderate‐certainty evidence that sucrose likely reduces pain from heel lance.

    How up‐to‐date is this evidence?

    This evidence is up‐to‐date as of February 2022.

    Study funding sources

    We did not identify any studies that received funding from industry.

    Visual summary

    Authors' conclusions

    Implications for practice

    We included 55 studies in this review. Sucrose compared to water, placebo, or no intervention probably results in a reduction of PIPP scores 30 and 60 seconds after single heel lances (moderate‐certainty evidence) in two meta‐analyses with seven and two studies, respectively. The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose alone compared to NNS, breastfeeding, laser acupuncture, and facilitated tucking, in reducing pain intensity. The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose plus NNS compared to NNS. Sucrose compared to glucose, expressed breast milk, and skin‐to‐skin care, shows little to no difference in pain scores. Very small doses of 24% sucrose (e.g. 0.1 mL) were efficacious in preterm infants (Stevens 2018). Since the review focused on a single heel lance, the effects of repeated use of sucrose on immediate and long‐term neurodevelopmental outcomes are unknown.

    Although some studies indicate there are no long‐term consequences of repeated use of sucrose, Ranger 2019 found that mice exposed to pain during the first week of life demonstrated poorer memory function regardless of sucrose administration, indicating that memory performance is not protected by sucrose administration. Repetitive use of sucrose in the absence of pain also contributed to poorer short‐term memory (Ranger 2019). Given these inconsistencies, the American Academy of Pediatrics has recommended that, when sucrose is used, it should be tracked and documented as a medication (AAP 2016; McPherson 2020).

    In addition to sucrose, other nonpharmacological approaches to managing procedural pain such as breastfeeding, skin‐to‐skin care, and facilitated tucking are recommended for incorporation into routine care whenever possible (Bucsea 2020; McNair 2019) and sucrose should be used when these parent‐initiated modalities are not possible (e.g. parent is not available). Also, two of the included studies combined the use of sucrose with adjuvant interventions (i.e. NNS and containment (Benoit 2021); and sucrose with swaddling (Velumula 2020)), which could contribute to overall pain reduction (Campbell‐Yeo 2022).

    Implications for research

    Stevens and colleagues identified 0.1 mL of 24% sucrose as the minimally effective dose to treat pain for single heel lances (Stevens 2018); however, this was only one study targeting one painful procedure and requires replicating to determine the optimal dose in other procedures with term and preterm infants. Researchers should consider more precise and tailored doses based on the infant (e.g. gestational age, PNA, birthweight), and the context (e.g. severity of illness) in which it is used. Optimal sucrose doses could be assessed further using sensitivity/meta‐regression techniques.

    More research is needed to better understand the underlying analgesic mechanisms and calming effects of sucrose (Eccleston 2021; McPherson 2020) and their relationship. Given recent results of animal studies on the long‐term neurodevelopmental effects of sucrose, further investigation of single and repeated doses is required to determine if sucrose is compromising or protective. The effects of sucrose in combination with other nonpharmacological (e.g. facilitated tucking, skin‐to‐skin care, breastfeeding), and pharmacological (e.g. morphine, fentanyl) interventions based on current systematic reviews (Johnston 2017; McNair 2019) need to be further studied in terms of additional benefits versus risk. However, the considerable variation in infant pain responses makes this integration of evidence difficult.

    Strengthened study design and methods with particular attention to the adequacy of sample size, conceptualisation of pain, use of validated pain measures to determine outcomes, and consideration of context are required. The revised IASP definition of pain takes pain in infants into consideration such that "verbal description is only one of several behaviours to express pain; inability to communicate does not negate the possibility that a human or a nonhuman animal experiences pain” and that pain and nociception are not the same (Raja 2020). Use of sucrose in neonates that are of extremely low birthweight, unstable, ventilated, or a combination of these factors still needs to be addressed for effectiveness and safety.

    Replication of existing studies of high methodological quality, using a valid pain assessment measure and standard set of validated outcomes would allow for further combination of results in meta‐analyses. Researchers should report means and standard deviations (SD) or standard error (SE) in addition to medians and ranges, if the data are not normally distributed, to allow for the use of meta‐analytic techniques. If researchers choose to present data in graph form, they should include the means and SD/SEs in the text (or in additional appendices). The total volume and concentration of sucrose administered should be reported in relation to body weight (g/kg). Future research should focus on the long‐term effects of repeated sucrose administration on biochemical as well as behavioural impacts in sick and very‐preterm neonates. Results should be presented for individual pain indicators and any composite pain scores constructed from these indicators. The relationship between pain measures and individual physiological and cognitive indicators of nociceptive brain activity should be explored further, factoring in differences in response times.

    In summary, there is a pressing need for research regarding the neurophysiological impact of pain, as well as the impact of sucrose delivered alone or in combination with other pain‐relieving strategies across repeated administrations on the developing brain.

    Effective knowledge translation (implementation and dissemination) strategies are required to translate research evidence on sucrose into practice effectively (Stevens 2014b). For healthcare providers, these strategies could include, in addition to the typical publications and presentations, the use of reminders, interactive education and educational outreach and regular audit, feedback sessions, and social media (e.g. Twitter, Facebook, infographics, webinars etc.).

    CI: confidence interval; MD: mean difference

    GRADE Working Group grades of evidence
    High certainty: we are very confident that the true effect lies close to that of the estimate of the effect.
    Moderate certainty: we are moderately confident in the effect estimate: the true effect is likely to be close to the estimate of the effect, but there is a possibility that it is substantially different.
    Low certainty: our confidence in the effect estimate is limited: the true effect may be substantially different from the estimate of the effect.
    Very low certainty: we have very little confidence in the effect estimate: the true effect is likely to be substantially different from the estimate of effect.

    See interactive version of this table: https://gdt.gradepro.org/presentations/#/isof/isof_question_revman_web_425799338500427522.

    b Downgraded one level for serious inconsistency (due to I² = 62%).
    c Downgraded one level for serious inconsistency (due to I² = 47%).
    d Downgraded one level due to wide confidence interval.
    e Downgraded two levels for very serious inconsistency (due to I² = 75%).
    f Douleur Aiguë du Nouveau‐né Scale (DAN).
    g Downgraded two levels due to concerns about performance and detection bias, and unclear risk of bias on allocation, attrition, and reporting bias.
    h Downgraded two levels due to wide confidence interval and small sample size.
    i Neonatal Infant Pain Scale (NIPS).
    j Pain scores were measured immediately after heel lancing according to authors’ description as reported in their study.

    Background

    Description of the condition

    Infants hospitalised in the neonatal intensive care unit (NICU) undergo frequent painful tissue‐damaging procedures for diagnostic and therapeutic purposes. Epidemiological research from audits in NICUs in high‐income countries estimates that neonate infants undergo an average of up to 17 painful exposures per day (Cruz 2016; Orovec 2019). Similarly, estimates of procedural pain in infants in low‐ and middle‐income countries ‐ including those in South America (Ramos 2019), Asia (Chen 2012; Jeong 2014), and Africa (Kyololo 2014), are equally high.

    Treatment for prevention or relief of procedural pain in the NICU varies widely in practice and is generally less than optimal (Carbajal 2008; Johnston 2011). An audit of 3508 tissue‐damaging procedures performed on 582 infants across a one‐week period in 14 Canadian NICUs indicated that only about 50% were accompanied by any form of pharmacological, behavioural or physical pain‐relieving intervention (Johnston 2011). More recently, another Canadian study reported a total of 10,469 painful procedures performed during the entire NICU stay of preterm infants; being pain relief interventions documented for only 58.5% (Orovec 2019). Although clinical practice guidelines outlining strategies to relieve pain from surgery, medical illness, and major procedures exist (Lee 2014), their quality is inconsistent.

    Untreated pain in neonates and, particularly, preterm infants, during a critical time in brain development, has the potential to result in significant immediate and long‐term consequences (Duerden 2018; Grunau 2013; Schwaller 2014; Walker 2013). These consequences include: (a) changes in somatosensory processing and altered sensitivity to future painful stimuli; (b) impaired neuro‐anatomical development (e.g. to thalamus – Duerden 2018); and, (c) behavioural, emotional and learning disabilities (Bartocci 2006; Brummelte 2012; Grunau 2013; Holsti 2005; Slater 2006; Tu 2007; Vinall 2014a; Vinall 2014b; Walker 2013; Zwicker 2013).

    Description of the intervention

    Administration of oral sucrose with or without non‐nutritive sucking (NNS) (e.g. pacifiers) and other sweet solutions (e.g. glucose) prior to and during heel lance have been the most frequently studied interventions for relief of procedural pain in neonate infants (Bueno 2013; Harrison 2017; Stevens 2016). Analgesic effects persist up to approximately one year of age, although the robustness of the effect may decline in older infants compared to neonates (Harrison 2013).

    How the intervention might work

    Analgesic, calming and stress‐reducing effects of sucrose were first reported in infant rats (Blass 1989; Ren 1997; Shide 1989). These effects occurred rapidly, persisted for several minutes and were blocked by systemic opioid receptor antagonists. They were age‐dependent and had differential maturational effects consistent with changes in the endogenous analgesic mechanisms and the development of the gustatory and pain pathways (Anseloni 2002). Researchers contend that sucrose may act at, or be mediated at, the level of the brainstem (Anseloni 2002; Anseloni 2005; Fitzgerald 2015), which has been shown to be a primary relay in the ascending gustatory pathway in animals (Anseloni 2005).

    In human infants, the analgesic and calming effects of sweet‐tasting solutions are speculated to influence endogenous opioid pathways activated by the sweet taste (Blass 1994). However, the underlying mechanisms for calming and pain may differ. These mechanisms may be additive or synergistic, but most likely depend on normal functioning of central mechanisms. The effects are associated with the potency of sweet taste (i.e. sweeter, more concentrated solutions), rather than volume of solution administered; with sucrose being more analgesic than glucose and fructose, and lactose not demonstrating any analgesic effects (Blass 1994). In a systematic review/meta‐analysis of the efficacy of sucrose for procedural pain management in 13 trials and 982 neonates, Stevens 1997a found that the proportion of time crying decreased with 240 mg to 480 mg sucrose (i.e. 2 mL of a 12% to 24% solution) administered orally two minutes prior to a painful procedure (e.g. heel lance or venipuncture).

    Despite advances on the potential mechanisms of sucrose analgesia, further research is required to enhance our understanding of the opioid pathways involved in the developing infant, the effectiveness of sucrose when administered with concomitant opioids or other pain‐relieving interventions, or both, and with repeated use over time (Harrison 2012).

    Why it is important to do this review

    This systematic review builds on the findings of previously published Cochrane reviews (Stevens 1998; Stevens 2001; Stevens 2004; Stevens 2010; Stevens 2013; Stevens 2016). In the 2016 review, 74 studies were included, with 7049 term and preterm infants, and results demonstrated that sucrose reduces different neonatal pain indicators alone and in composite scores of neonatal pain intensity during heel lance, venipuncture, and intramuscular injection (Stevens 2016). Tissue‐damaging procedures included heel lance, venipuncture, arterial puncture, intramuscular injections, echocardiography, peripheral venous catheter insertion, suctioning, eye examinations, circumcision, subcutaneous injections, bladder catheterisation, and nasogastric tube insertion. Since heel lance is the most commonly performed painful procedure in neonates as well as the most frequently investigated procedure in randomised controlled trials (RCTs), the authors decided to focus specifically on the effects of sucrose on heel lance for this current 2022 review (see Differences between protocol and review).

    Repeated use, or use in extremely preterm and sick infants, has rarely been addressed (Harrison 2009). Johnston reported that preterm infants of less than 31 weeks' gestational age who were exposed to more than 10 repeated doses of sucrose per day were more prone to poorer attention and motor development in early life (Johnston 2002; Johnston 2007). Other studies have not reported differences between sucrose and non‐sucrose groups (Banga 2016; Gaspardo 2008; Stevens 2005b; Taddio 2009a). However, comprehensive studies evaluating repeated dosing of sucrose for all painful procedures during hospitalisations of the neonate have not been undertaken.

    Concerns regarding the repeated use of sucrose and cumulative volumes administered during painful procedures in the developing brains of preterm infants have been raised (McPherson 2020).

    Although 24% or 25% sucrose solutions are the most widely used concentrations for the treatment of procedural pain in clinical practice, there is considerable variation in the volumes of sucrose reported to be effective in diminishing pain, and a more than 20‐fold variation in doses used in clinical practice (Taddio 2009a). There is currently no clear recommendation regarding the optimal volume of sucrose for analgesia in infants, or for the use of sucrose in the presence of opioid analgesia, despite administration of opioids to infants during their NICU stay (Harrison 2009; Johnston 2011). Ideally, the amount of sucrose (mg/kg body weight) administered should be reported. A critical evaluation of guidelines for incorporating sucrose as an intervention for preventing or ameliorating procedural pain in infants indicates that recommendations for practitioners are not clear (Lee 2014). These knowledge gaps and lack of consistent strategies for effective application of knowledge most likely contribute to the current suboptimal or non‐standardised utilisation of sucrose in NICUs.

    Objectives

    To assess the effectiveness of sucrose for relieving the pain of heel lance in neonate infants in terms of immediate (i.e. validated pain scores, neurophysiological and behavioural pain indicators) and long‐term (neurodevelopmental) outcomes

    Methods

    Criteria for considering studies for this review

    Types of studies

    We considered RCTs that evaluated the effect of sucrose analgesia in neonate infants undergoing single or repeated heel lances. We did not include quasi‐randomised trials. Conference abstracts were not included as we have identified discrepancies in numbers of infants enrolled between abstracts and final publications (Walia 1999). We did not impose language restrictions.

    Types of participants

    We included studies that assessed term, preterm, or both term and preterm neonate infants, with maximum postnatal age (PNA) of 28 days after reaching 40 weeks' postmenstrual age (PMA).

    Types of interventions

    Interventions:

    • Oral sucrose in any concentration or dose, administered via any method.

    • Oral sucrose in any concentration or dose, administered via any method with non‐nutritive sucking (NNS), e.g. pacifier.

    Comparisons:

    a. water, placebo or no intervention;

    b. analgesia;

    c. NNS with or without water;

    d. glucose (any concentration, any dose);

    e. breastfeeding;

    f. breast milk or milk formula;

    g. recorded music;

    h. laser acupuncture;

    i. facilitated tucking;

    j. skin‐to‐skin care.

    Types of outcome measures

    The outcomes are organised as follows.

    Primary outcomes

    Pain intensity scores measured by validated instruments such as:

    • Premature Infant Pain Profile (PIPP; Stevens 1996);

    • Premature Infant Pain Profile‐Revised (PIPP‐R; Stevens 2014a);

    • Douleur Aiguë du Nouveau‐né Scale (DAN; Carbajal 1997);

    • Neonatal Infant Pain Scale (NIPS; Lawrence 1993);

    • Neonatal Facial Coding System (NFCS; Grunau 1987);

    • Neonatal Pain Agitation and Sedation Scale (N‐PASS; Hummel 2010);

    • Bernese Pain Scale for Neonates (BPSN; Cignacco 2004);

    • COMFORTneo Scale (Van Dijk 2009);

    • others as reported in trial.

    We included pain intensity scores assessed at:

    • time of the procedure;

    • after the procedure;

    • intervals as reported in the study (e.g. 30 seconds after heel lance, 60 seconds after heel lance, etc.).

    Secondary outcomes
    Individual behavioural pain indicators

    • Cry (duration of first cry in seconds, total duration of cry in seconds, proportion of time crying, etc.);

    • Facial actions (presence or absence of facial actions, time in seconds facial actions were present, etc.).

    Individual neurophysiological pain indicators

    • Heart rate (in beats per minute (bpm), percentage of change in HR, etc.);

    • Respiratory rate (in respirations per minute (rpm), percentage of change in RR, etc.);

    • Oxygen saturation of the blood (in %, percentage of change in oxygen saturation, etc.);

    • TcpO2 (in Pascal ‐ Pa, kilopascal ‐ kPa, or millimetres of mercury ‐ mmHg);

    • TcpCO2 (in Pascal ‐ Pa, kilopascal ‐ kPa, or millimetres of mercury ‐ mmHg);

    • Cortisol level (ug/dL);

    • NIRS;

    • EEG.

    Long‐term neurodevelopmental outcomes (assessed by a standardised and validated assessment tool, a child developmental specialist, or both) at 18 to 24 months, or at any later age in childhood.

    Any adverse effects reported.

    Search methods for identification of studies

    Electronic searches

    Searches were run in February 2022 and were conducted without date, language or publication type limits. We acknowledge the date of search is greater than one year old; this resulted from editorial delays related to COVID and staffing issues at the Cochrane Neonatal editorial base.

    The following databases were searched:

    • Cochrane Library, Wiley, Issue 2, 2022 (searched February 23, 2022);

    • Ovid MEDLINE® ALL 1946 to February 23, 2022;

    • Embase 1980 to 2022 Week 07 (Ovid);

    • PsycINFO 1806 to February Week 2 2022 (Ovid);

    • CINAHL Complete (EBSCOhost) (searched February 23, 2022);

    • Web of Science Core Collection (Clarivate), (searched 1982‐February 23, 2022).

    The search strategies for each database are reported in Appendix 1; Appendix 2; Appendix 3; Appendix 4; Appendix 5; Appendix 6.

    Searching other resources

    The following trials registers resources were searched on February 23, 2022 using their native, web‐based interfaces.

    • US National Library of Medicine https://clinicaltrials.gov;

    • EU Clinical Trials Register (https://www.clinicaltrialsregister.eu);

    • WHO International Clinical Trials Registers Platform (ICTRP) (https://trialsearch.who.int).

    The search strategies are reported in Appendix 7.

    Data collection and analysis

    Selection of studies

    Titles and abstracts were independently screened by two reviewers (MB, LS and MB, SH) and disagreements were resolved by discussion or a third reviewer (JY).

    Data extraction and management

    Two reviewers (LS, MB) extracted data separately using predesigned forms. We compared the data and resolved differences using a third reviewer (JY). We had additional data provided by investigators for three studies (Harrison 2003; Johnston 1999; Stevens 1999) that were previously included in another review (Stevens 2016).

    Assessment of risk of bias in included studies

    Two reviewers (LS, MB), independently assessed the risk of bias (low, high, or unclear) of all included trials using the Cochrane Risk of bias tool (Higgins 2017), for the following domains:

    • random sequence generation (selection bias);

    • allocation concealment (selection bias);

    • blinding of participants and personnel (performance bias and detection bias);

    • blinding of outcome assessment (detection bias);

    • incomplete outcome data (attrition bias);

    • selective reporting (reporting bias);

    • any other bias.

    We resolved any disagreements by discussion or by a third assessor (JY). See Appendix 8 for a more detailed description of risk of bias for each domain. 

    Measures of treatment effect

    We performed statistical analyses using Review Manager 5 (RevMan 5) (RevMan Web 2020). We analysed categorical data using risk ratio (RR), risk difference (RD) and the number needed to treat for an additional beneficial outcome (NNTB), or the number needed to treat for an additional harmful outcome (NNTH) if the RD was statistically significant. We analysed continuous data using mean difference (MD). We reported the 95% confidence interval (CI) on all estimates. Following advice from Dr Michael Bracken (statistician of Cochrane Neonatal) we decided not to convert medians and interquartile or full ranges to means and SDs.

    Unit of analysis issues

    We did not identify any cluster‐randomised trials and so did not encounter any unit of analysis issues.

    Dealing with missing data

    Many authors did not report means and SDs for the outcomes. We transformed 95% CIs to SDs using the techniques described under Measures of treatment effect. Many studies reported the results in graph form only, and we could not incorporate the findings in RevMan analyses.

    Assessment of heterogeneity

    We report the I2 statistic for all analyses in which more than one trial was included. We categorised the heterogeneity using the following labels and cutoffs for the results of the I2 test; less than 25%, no heterogeneity present; 25% to 49%, low heterogeneity; 50% to 74%, moderate heterogeneity; and 75% or more, high heterogeneity. If we detected statistical heterogeneity (I2 = 50%), we explored the possible causes (e.g. differences in study quality, participants (term or preterm infants), intervention regimens or outcome assessments) using post hoc subgroup analyses.

    Assessment of reporting biases

    We planned to construct forest plots if there were at least 10 studies included in one meta‐analysis that assessed the same outcome in comparable trials of the same intervention in the same population; however, no single meta‐analysis fulfilled this criterion.

    Data synthesis

    We used the statistical package Review Manager 5 (RevMan 5) provided by Cochrane (RevMan Web 2020). For meta‐analyses, we reported an MD with 95% CI using a fixed‐effect model for continuous outcome measures. For categorical outcomes, we reported the typical RR, RD and, if the RD was statistically significant, we planned to report the NNTB or NNTH. All values were reported with their 95% CIs.

    Subgroup analysis and investigation of heterogeneity

    We did not make separate subgroup comparisons.

    Sensitivity analysis

    Under each comparison for the outcomes, we reported the results in the term and the preterm populations separately and combined these whenever data were available.

    Summary of findings and assessment of the certainty of the evidence

    We used the GRADE approach, as outlined in the GRADE Handbook (Schünemann 2013), to assess the certainty of evidence of the following (clinically relevant) outcome:

    • validated pain score

    Two review authors (SH, MB) independently assessed the certainty of the evidence for the outcome above. We considered evidence from RCTs as high certainty but downgraded the evidence one level for serious (or two levels for very serious) limitations based upon the following: design (risk of bias), consistency across studies, directness of the evidence, precision of estimates, and presence of publication bias. We used the GRADEpro GDT to create nine Summary of findings tables to report the certainty of the evidence.

    The GRADE approach results in an assessment of the certainty of a body of evidence as one of four grades.

    • High certainty: further research is very unlikely to change our confidence in the estimate of effect.

    • Moderate certainty: further research is likely to have an important impact on our confidence in the estimate of effect and may change the estimate.

    • Low certainty: further research is very likely to have an important impact on our confidence in the estimate of effect and is likely to change the estimate.

    • Very low certainty: we are very uncertain about the estimate.

    Results

    Description of studies

    See: Characteristics of included studies; Characteristics of excluded studies; Characteristics of studies awaiting classification; Characteristics of ongoing studies

    Results of the search

    Searches of databases and trial registries identified 16,896 references; after removing 9721 duplicates, 7175 records were available for screening. We excluded 7057 based on title/abstract and reviewed 118 full‐texts or trial registry records. We included 55 studies; excluded 57 studies; allocated two studies to awaiting classification; and identified four ongoing trials. Details are available in Figure 1.

    • Open in figure viewer
    Figure 1

    Study flow diagram: review update

    Included studies

    We included a total of 55 studies (enrolling 6273 infants) in this systematic review.

    Of the 55 included studies, 42 were included in our previous, broader review of multiple painful procedures, Sucrose for analgesia in newborn infants undergoing painful procedures (Stevens 2016); and 13 are newly identified studies (Benoit 2021; Campbell‐Yeo 2019Chang 2020; Gao 2018; Kumari 2017; Lago 2020; McGinnis 2016; Mitchell 2016; Nimbalkar 2020; Sen 2020; Shah 2017; Stevens 2018; Velumula 2020). The largest study included in this review reported results on 671 infants (Leng 2015).

    Twenty‐nine of the 55 included studies focused on term infants only (Abbasoglu 2015; Altun‐Köroğlu 2010; Benoit 2021; Blass 1997; Blass 1999; Chang 2020; Codipietro 2008; Gormally 2001; Greenberg 2002; Guala 2001; Haouari 1995; Isik 2000a; Leng 2013; Leng 2015; Marin Gabriel 2013; Mathai 2006; McGinnis 2016; Mitchell 2016; Ogawa 2005; Ors 1999; Overgaard 1999; Ramenghi 1996a; Rushforth 1993; Slater 2010; Taddio 2008; Thakkar 2016; Tutag Lehr 2015; Unceta‐Barranechea 2008; Yilmaz 2010), while 22 included preterm infants only (Asmerom 2013; Bucher 1995; Campbell‐Yeo 2019; Cignacco 2012; Gao 2018; Gaspardo 2008; Harrison 2003; Johnston 1997; Johnston 1999; Johnston 2002; Kumari 2017; Lago 2020; Nimbalkar 2020; Okan 2007; Ramenghi 1996b; Ramenghi 1999; Sen 2020; Simonse 2012; Stevens 1999; Stevens 2005a; Storm 2002; Velumula 2020), and four included both preterm and term infants (Gibbins 2002; Liaw 2013; Shah 2017; Stevens 2018). Details of each study are outlined in Table 1; Table 2; Table 3.

    Abbasoglu 2015

    42 term newborns, undergoing heel lancing between postnatal days 3 and 8 as part of neonatal screening

    Heel lance

    • Laser acupuncture: 0.3 J of energy was applied to the Yintang point using a Laser PREMIO‐30 unit for 30 s

    • Sucrose: 0.5 mL 24% solution was given orally via syringe 2 min before heel lancing

    • NIPS

    • Crying time

    • Adverse events

    Mean and SD

    The NIPS score was lower in the sucrose group than in the laser group (3.66 ± 1.01 vs 4.52 ± 0.87; P = 0.006).

    The crying time was lower in the sucrose group than in the laser group (46.66 s ± 37.82 s vs 97.95 s ± 34.23 s; P = 0.000).

    No infant developed any clinically visible changes on the skin, and no side effects were observed in their comparison of laser acupuncture with oral sucrose.

    Data are included in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: not specified

    Altun‐Köroğlu 2010

    75 full‐term infants

    Heel lance

    • 3 mL hindmilk (n = 25)

    • 3 mL 12.5% sucrose solution (n = 25)

    • 3 mL distilled water (n = 25)

    The volume of 3 mL was provided in 3 doses (1 mL prior to, 1 mL immediately before, and 1 mL after the procedure)

    • NFCS

    • Crying time

    • Duration of crying

    • HR

    Median and IQR

    Median crying time, duration of first cry and tachycardia, and time needed to return to baseline were longest in the distilled water group and shorter in the hindmilk group when compared to distilled water group (P = 0.022, P = 0.008, P = 0.009 and P = 0.038, respectively).

    No differences were observed between the hindmilk and sucrose group for all measures.

    Maximum HR in hindmilk group was lower than in distilled water group (184 beats/min vs 196 beats/min, P = 0.031).

    Reduction in average NFCS score. First‐minute NFCS score and fifth‐minute NFCS scores were lower in the hindmilk group compared to the distilled water group (P = 0.006, P = 0.017 and P = 0.021, respectively).

    No data were included in RevMan analyses.

    Asmerom 2013

    131 preterm infants ≤ 36.5 weeks' gestation who:

    • weighed ≥ 800 g;

    • had a central catheter in place; and

    • required a heel lance

    Heel lance

    • Placebo + NNS (n = 45)

    • 42 infants received no heel lance, no sucrose, or placebo.

    • PIPP after 2 min

    • Plasma hypoxanthine

    • Uric acid

    • Xanthine

    • Allantoin

    • HR

    • SpO2

    Mean and lowest and highest values

    Oral sucrose given before a single heel lance decreased behavioural markers of pain.
    Sucrose increased markers of ATP use, as evidenced by increases over time in plasma hypoxanthine and uric acid concentrations.
    We received the PIPP scores as means and SD from Dr D Angeles, and the data were included in RevMan analyses.

    Benoit 2021

    39 healthy full‐term (born ≥ 37 0/7 weeks' gestation)

    Heel lance

    • BF (n = 20)

    • 24% sucrose (0.24 mL) + NNS + containment in a blanket while in an infant cot 2 min prior to heel lance (n = 19)

    • Pain‐related potential was recorded on electroencephalogram

    • PIPP‐R at 30 s, 90 s, 120 s after heel lance

    • Physiologic recovery

    • Adverse events

    PIPP‐R scores: t‐tests were reported; no means or SD

    Infants in BF group had a smaller, but not different (F[1,15.9] = 0.58, P = 0.64, SE = 11.79), amplitude pain‐related potential (peak amplitude 0.29 μV) following heel lance compared to infants who received oral sucrose (peak amplitude 8.97 μV). Mean PIPP‐R scores were not different between groups following heel lance, however, but represented low pain across groups. Mean time in seconds to physiologic recovery was faster in BF group (M = 17.5, SD = 31.1) compared to oral sucrose group (M = 70.8, SD = 144.3).

    There were no adverse events or safety concerns in either group.

    Blass 1999

    40 term newborn infants, 34 h to 55 h old

    Heel lance

    Prior to heel lance:

    • 2 mL 12% sucrose over 2 min via syringe (n = 10);

    • 2 mL water via syringe over 2 min (n = 10);

    • pacifier dipped every 30 s in 12% sucrose solution for 2 min (n = 10); and

    • pacifier dipped in water every 30 s for 2 min (n = 10)

    • Percentage of time crying 3 min after heel lance

    • Mean change in HR

    • Percentage of time grimacing

    • Mean percentage

    • Mean change (beats/min)

    • Graphically reported

    2 mL 12% (0.24 g) sucrose alone diminished cry duration from heel lance compared to water (8% vs 50%, P = 0.003) and water with pacifier (8% vs 35%, P = 0.002). Pacifier with 12% sucrose was more effective in reducing cry duration compared to water with pacifier (5% vs 35%, P = 0.001) or water alone (50%, P = 0.002).

    Mean HR increased from treatment to heel lance in infants receiving water alone (mean increase of 17 beats/min, P = 0.002) and water with pacifier (mean increase of 20 beats/min, P = 0.005). Mean increase in HR also increased for the 12% sucrose and pacifier group (mean difference of 7.4 beats/min, P = 0.05) but not for infants receiving 12% sucrose alone (mean difference of 5.9 beats/min, P = 0.142).

    12% sucrose reduced grimacing compared to water (P = 0.0003). 12% sucrose with pacifier reduced grimacing compared to water (P = 0.001) and pacifier alone (P = 0.04).

    No data could be used in RevMan analyses.

    Bucher 1995

    16 preterm infants, 27 weeks' to 34 weeks' GA, PNA approximately 42 d

    Heel lance

    • 2 mL 50% sucrose via syringe into the mouth 2 min before heel lance

    • 2 mL distilled water via syringe into the mouth 2 min before heel lance (n = 16, cross‐over design)

    • Percentage of time crying

    • Recovery time until crying stopped

    • Increase in HR

    • TcPO2 (maximum increase, measured in kPa; maximum decrease, in kPa; difference between baseline and 10 min after end of intervention ‐ kPa);

    Median, IQR

    Median increase in HR (beats/min) after heel lance was reduced in the 2 mL 50% (1.0 g) sucrose group (35 beats/min) compared to water (51 beats/min) (P = 0.005).

    No differences between groups with respect to measures for TcPO2 (P = 0.05) and TcPCO2 (P = 0.21)

    Chang 2020

    226 term infants

    Heel lance at > 24 h old when receiving the heel lance procedure as a newborn
    screen test

    • BF group (n = 45), initiated before heel lance and during heel lance procedure

    • Oral sucrose group (n = 42), 24% oral sucrose‐5 drops (0.33 mL) before heel lance and again during procedure if cues of pain, up to 15 drops (1 mL)

    • NNS group (n = 51), pacifier given before and during heel lance

    • SSC group (n = 38), newborns wore only diapers, positioned on mother's bare chest immediately before heel lance procedure

    • Control group (n = 50), newborns were placed in a crib in supine position and covered with blankets

    N‐PASS scores (duration of the heel lance was calculated from the point of lancet puncture until blood collection completion); N‐PASS scores were collected at these same procedure time points

    Mean, SD

    All intervention groups showed decreased pain levels compared with the control group (P < 0.01). The oral sucrose group experienced a superior analgesic effect compared to the SSC group (P < 0.01), but there was no difference when compared with the BF group
    (P > 0.05) or the NNS group (P > 0.05).

    All intervention groups had shortened crying times (P < 0.01) and reduced procedural duration (P < 0.01) compared with the control group.

    Cignacco 2012

    71 preterm infants between 24 0/7 weeks' and 32 0/7 weeks' PMA

    Repeated heel lances

    • Sucrose group (n = 24): 20% oral sucrose (0.2 mL/kg), ∼2 min before the heel lance. If the infant seemed to be in pain during the heel lance phase, up to 2 additional doses of sucrose were administered and noted in the study chart.

    • Sucrose + FT group (n = 23): FT was started at the beginning of the baseline phase, and sucrose was given 2 min before the heel lance.

    • FT group (n = 24): FT was started at the beginning of the baseline phase, and the infant was 'tucked' through all 3 phases.

    BPSN. Data collection occurred during:

    • baseline (before any manipulation);

    • heel lance (skin preparation, heel stick and haemostasis after blood was drawn); and

    • recovery (3 min after the heel lance).

    The BPSN contains 9 items: 3 physiologic (HR, RR and SpO2) and 6 behavioural (grimacing, body movements, crying, skin colour, sleeping patterns, consolation) items.

    3 phases (baseline, heel stick, recovery) of 5 heel stick procedures were videotaped for each infant.

    Mean, SD

    Sucrose with and without FT had pain‐relieving effects even in preterm infants of GA ≤ 32 weeks having repeated pain exposures. These interventions remained effective during repeated heel sticks across time. FT was not as effective.

    Data were included in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: Swiss National Science Foundation and the following private

    the Foundation of Nursing Science, Basel; the Foundation for Research in

    Neonatology, Zürich; the Marie Anna Foundation, Basel; and the Foundation

    for Sick Children, Basel

    Declaration of interest: The authors indicated they had no financial relationships relevant to this article to disclose.

    Codipietro 2008

    101 term infants

    Mean PMA:

    • sucrose group: 39.3 weeks; and

    • BF group: 39.4 weeks

    Heel lance

    • 1 mL 25% sucrose via syringe (n = 50)

    • BF prior to heel lance (n = 51)

    • Duration of first cry (s), percentage of crying time in first 2 min, and percentage of crying time during blood sampling

    • HR (beats/min) increase from baselines at 30 s following commencement of procedure

    • SpO2 decrease

    • PIPP during blood sampling, 2 min after heel lance

    • Adverse events

    Median, range

    Percentage of crying during first 2 min: BF group, 4% (range 0% to 100%) compared to sucrose, 45% (range 0% to 100%) (P < 0.0001)

    Percentage of crying during sampling: BF group, 8% (range 0% to 100%) compared to sucrose, 56.5% (range 0% to 100%) (P = 0.0003)

    Median increase in HR from baseline to 30 s after start of heel lance was lower in BF group (13 beats/min; range 12 beats/min to 54 beats/min) compared to sucrose group (22 beats/min; range 32 to 65) (P = 0.005)

    Gao 2018

    86 preterm infants born before 37 weeks

    Heel lance

    • Routine care (routine comfort through gentle touch when infants cried; n = 21)

    • NNS (n = 22) 2 min before and during recovery of heel lance

    • 1 mL sucrose (0.2 mL/kg of 20%; n = 21) 2 min before heel lance

    • 1 mL sucrose (0.2 mL/kg of 20%) + NNS (n = 22) 2 min before heel lance

    • PIPP score (during heel lance, 1 min after heel lance)

    • HR (baseline = 2 min before, during, 1 min after heel lance)

    • SpO2 (baseline = 2 min before, during, 1 min after heel lance)

    • Crying time (baseline = 2 min before, during, 1 min after heel lance)

    • Incidence of adverse events

      • Choking, coughing, vomiting, abdominal distension, oral infection, necrotising enterocolitis

      • Sustained tachycardia (HR > 200 beats/min), bradycardia (HR < 80 beats/min), tachypnoea (RR > 80 beats/min), dyspnoea (RR < 20 beats/min), or oxygen desaturation < 80% for > 15 s

      • hyperglycaemia

    Mean, SD

    The combination group had lower PIPP scores, HR, and crying time during the heel lance and recovery phase than the sucrose, NNS and routine care groups (P < 0.0001).

    The combination group had a higher SpO2 than all other treatment groups (P < 0.035).

    There were no differences in the HR and SpO2 between the sucrose, NNS and routine care groups.

    The sucrose group and NNS group had similar duration of crying, and the routine care group had the longest duration of crying of all treatment groups.

    3 episodes of vomiting (1 in the routine care group, 1 in the NNS group and 1 in the sucrose + NNS group) and 2 infants who had abdominal distension (1 in the sucrose + NNS group and 1 in the routine care group) were reported.

    Data were included in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: National Natural

    Science Foundation of China (81703246), the Preponderant Discipline Project of Universities in Jiangsu Province, Nursing Science Open Fund of Nanjing University of Chinese Medicine (YSHL2016‐018), Top‐notch Academic Programs Project of Jiangsu Higher Education Institutions (PPZY2015C258) and Project of Nursing Science in Nanjing University of Chinese Medicine (NZYHLXPPJG2017‐54)

    Gibbins 2002

    190 preterm and term infants, mean GA 33.7 weeks, < 7 d PNA

    Heel lance

    2 min prior to heel lance:

    • sucrose + NNS group: 0.5 mL 24% sucrose via syringe to the anterior surface of the tongue followed by pacifier (n = 64);

    • sucrose group: 0.5 mL 24% sucrose without pacifier (n = 62); and

    • water + NNS group: 0.5 mL sterile water with pacifier (n = 64)

    • PIPP scores at 30 s and 60 s after heel lance

    • Incidence of adverse events

    Reported means, SD

    Mean PIPP scores at both 30 s (P < 0.001) and 60 s (P < 0.001) after heel lance favoured sucrose group and sucrose + NNS group. Post hoc Tukey tests showed infants who received sucrose + pacifier had lower PIPP scores after heel lance at 30 s (mean 8.16, SD 3.24) compared to infants receiving sucrose alone (mean 9.77, SD 3.04) (P = 0.007) or water + NNS (mean 10.19, SD 2.67 (P < 0.001). At 60 s after heel lance, PIPP scores were lower for sucrose + NNS group (mean 8.78, SD 4.03) compared to the sucrose alone group (mean 11.20, SD 3.25) (P = 0.005) and water + NNS group (mean 11.20, SD 3.47 (P = 0.007). No differences in PIPP scores were found between sucrose alone group or water + NNS group at both follow‐up times.

    Minor adverse effects were observed in 6 infants, none of which occurred in the sucrose + NNS group. 1 neonate who received water with NNS choked when the water was administered but stabilised within 10 s. 3 infants randomised to the sucrose group and 2 infants randomised to the water with NNS groups experienced oxygen desaturation when the study intervention (sucrose or water) was administered. Each neonate recovered spontaneously with no medical intervention required.

    Data were used in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: Hospital for Sick Children, Toronto Ontario, Canada for the Research Training Award

    Declaration of interest: not specified

    Gormally 2001

    94 term newborns, mean GA 39.4 weeks, second or third day of life

    Heel lance

    • Water group: no holding and sterile water given by pipette (n = 21)

    • Sucrose group: no holding and 0.25 mL 24% sucrose solution given by pipette (n = 22)

    • Holding + water group: holding and sterile water given by pipette (n = 20)

    • Holding + sucrose group: holding and 0.25 mL 24% sucrose solution by pipette (n = 22)

    All solutions given 3 times at 30‐second intervals

    • Percentage of time crying 1 min, 2 min and 3 min after heel lance

    • Mean HR before intervention and 1 min, 2 min and 3 min after heel lance; mean vagal tone index before intervention and 1 min, 2 min and 3 min after heel lance

    • Pain concentration scores for facial activity before intervention and 1 min, 2 min and 3 min after heel lance

    Not reported

    Crying decreased over time (P < 0.001), but no interaction was noted for time with holding, taste or both. Effect of taste on crying (P < 0.05) favoured sucrose group. No effect of holding (P = 0.09). No interaction between taste and holding to reduce crying (P = 0.37). Effect of combined interventions was additive.

    Although no change in mean HR due to holding or sucrose as main effects, there was an interaction between holding and taste (P < 0.004), indicating synergistic effect that was also dependent on pre‐intervention HR (P < 0.004). No main effects noted for vagal tone; as with HR, effect of vagal tone was dependent on pre‐intervention vagal tone for both holding and taste interventions (P < 0.03). Pre‐intervention levels interacted to decrease HR and vagal tone in infants who had higher rates before interventions.

    Pain concatenation scores measuring facial expressions of pain decreased over time (P < 0.001). Only the effect of holding reduced pain scores (P < 0.02). No difference as to whether infant received sucrose (taste main effect P = 0.68)

    No data could be used in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: Medical Research Council of Canada (MT‐13633), the Allan Ross Fellowship and the Lewis Sessenwein Academic Award

    Harrison 2003

    99 sick hospitalised infants

    Mean GA (SD):

    • sucrose group: 36.7 weeks (3.3); and

    • control group: 36.8 weeks (3.7)

    (Author provided data on a subset of infants from a larger study (n = 128) that fulfilled our inclusion criteria)

    Heel lance

    • 1 mL water (n = 46)

    • 1 mL 25% sucrose (n = 53)

    For infants weighing ≤ 1500 g, the dose was reduced to 0.5 mL.

    • Duration of cry until 5‐second pause; incidence and duration of crying time during the heel lance and squeeze and during the 3‐min recovery period

    • HR at baseline; heel lance; during heel lance; and 1 min, 2 min and 3 min post‐heel lance

    • SpO2 (%) at baseline; heel lance; during heel lance; and 1 min, 2 min and 3 min post‐heel lance

    • 4‐point subset of the NFCS (brow bulge, eye squeeze, nasolabial furrow, stretch mouth) at heel lance; during heel lance; and 1 min, 2 min and 3 min post‐heel lance

    Mean, SD (collected from authors)

    Mean (SD) length of first cry was higher in the water group (70.5 s (83.6 s)) compared to the sucrose group (46.8 s (63.1 s)). The sucrose group cried 57.1% of the procedure time compared to 58.8% in the water group. The mean (SD) total duration of cry during the heel lance was 84.7 s (68.8 s) in the sucrose group and 87.4 s (87.1 s) in the water group.

    The mean (SD) HR upon heel lance was 163.0 beats/min (17.9 beats/min) in the sucrose group and 159.5 beats/min (19.2 beats/min) in the water group. HR at 30 s from the beginning of the procedure was 175.4 beats/min (22.2 beats/min) and 172.8 beats/min (23.6 beats/min) in the sucrose and water groups, respectively. The HR in both groups decreased after the procedure to 152.1 beats/min (22.5 beats/min) in the sucrose group and 154.2 beats/min (29.1 beats/min) in the water group 2 min post‐heel lance.

    Results of SpO2 (%) were similar between the 2 groups.

    Mean (SD) facial scores were reduced at heel lance (2.74 (1.8)) in the sucrose group compared to the water group (2.94 (1.6)) (P = 0.02) and at 1 min (P = 0.04) and 2 min (P = 0.046) post‐heel lance. No differences occurred at 3 min post‐heel lance.

    Data were included in RevMan analyses.

    Infants also received NNS and FT.

    Leng 2013

    560 term neonates, GA 37 weeks to 42 weeks; birthweight 2500 g to 4000 g; age 3 d to 28 d

    Heel lance

    • Boiled water

    • 10% glucose

    • 25% glucose

    • 50% glucose

    • 12% sucrose

    • 24% sucrose

    • 30% sucrose

    • Increase in HR (beats/min)

    • Decrease in SpO2 (%)

    • Pain levels (test not specified)

    • Adverse events

    Reported means and full ranges, not IQRs

    The average HR increase 3 min, 5 min and 10 min after procedure in the 25% and 50% glucose groups, 12% and 24% and 30% sucrose groups was lower than those in the placebo group (P < 0.01 or 0.05).     Leng 2015

    671 term neonates, GA 37 weeks to 42 weeks at birth; PNA 3 d to 28 d; birthweight 2500 g to 4000 g; Apgar score ≥ 8 at 5 min after birth; resting HR 120 beats/min to 140 beats/min; resting O2 saturation ≥ 95%; required neonatal congenital metabolism disease screening or blood glucose test

    Shallow (blood glucose test) and deep (congenital metabolism disease screening) heel lance

    • Sucrose group: 2 mL 24% sucrose administered to the infant’s mouth by syringe 2 min before the heel lance procedure

    • Sucrose + NNS group: 2 mL 24% sucrose administered as above, then a standard silicone newborn pacifier was placed into the infant’s mouth until the end of the process.

    • Sucrose + swaddling group: infants were swaddled with a cotton blanket, upper but not lower limb movements were restricted by the blanket, and then 2 mL 24% sucrose were administered as above. The lower limbs were swaddled immediately after the heel lance procedure until the end of the process.

    • Sucrose + NNS + swaddling group: infants were swaddled with a cotton blanket, upper but not lower limb movements were restricted by the blanket, then 2 mL 24% sucrose administered as above, then a standard silicone newborn pacifier was placed into the infant’s mouth, the lower limbs were swaddled immediately after the heel lance procedure until the end of the process.

    • Revised NFCS

    • Increase in HR (%)

    • Decrease in SpO2 (%)

    • Factor analysis

    • Mean and SD

    A synergistic analgesic effect was observed between the sucrose + NNS and sucrose + swaddling groups in both the shallow (P = 0.015) and deep heel lance (P = 0.007) procedure. The sucrose + NNS + swaddling group exhibited the lowest pain score. For the deep heel lance procedure, the sucrose + NNS group had a lower increase in HR percentage and decrease in SpO2 (%) than the sucrose group (P = 0.000, P = 0.001), while this difference was not observed in the shallow heel lance procedure. No difference was found between the sucrose and sucrose + swaddling groups, in terms of different physiological parameters;

    NNS and swaddling had synergistic effects on pain relief when used with oral sucrose. For the deep heel lance procedure, oral sucrose combined with NNS and swaddling provided the best pain relief effect.

    For the shallow heel lance procedure, addition of NNS and swaddling did not improve the effects.

    Data could not be used in RevMan analyses.

    of China, No [2013]544 and Medical Research Funding of Health Bureau of Chongqing, No [2011‐1‐071]

    Nimbalkar 2020

    100 preterm neonates (28 0/7 weeks' to 36 6/7 weeks' GA)

    Okan 2007

    31 healthy, preterm newborns; mean GA 30.5 weeks; mean PMA 32.3 weeks

    Heel lance

    • 2 mL 20% sucrose

    • 2 mL 20% glucose

    • 2 mL water

    The solutions were administered via syringe onto the anterior portion of the tongue.

    Cross‐over study – infants received all 3 interventions at different times.

    Data from the first treatment in each infant could not be derived.

    • Duration of first cry

    • Total crying time

    • HR, SpO2 (%)

    • RR (breaths/min)

    • NFCS scores (baseline; during heel lance; and 1 min, 2 min, 3 min, 4 min and 5 min post‐heel lance)

    Mean, SD

    Increased duration of first cry and total crying time in the water group compared to the sucrose and glucose groups (P = 0.005 and P = 0.007, respectively)

    No differences in cry characteristics were observed between the sucrose and glucose groups.

    Higher HR in the water group (mean 175 beats/min, SD 20.8 beats/min) compared to the sucrose (mean 166 beats/min, SD 17.6 beats/min) and glucose groups (mean 165 beats/min, SD 17.5 beats/min) at 1 min following heel lance (P = 0.007). No differences between the sucrose and glucose groups

    The study found no differences for RR or SpO2 between groups.

    Higher NFCS score in the placebo group in the fourth minute following heel lance (mean 1.3, SD 2.0) and fifth min following heel lance (mean 1.0, SD 1.0) compared to the sucrose (mean 0.5, SD 1.7; mean 0.3, SD 1.3, respectively) and glucose groups (mean 0.2, SD 0.5; mean 0.1, SD 0.3, respectively) (P = 0.009 at fourth minute and P = 0.046 at fifth minute). There were no differences between the sucrose and glucose groups.

    Data could not be used in RevMan analyses.

    Overgaard 1999

    100 newborn term infants, mean PNA 6 d (range 4 d to 9 d)

    Heel lance

    • 2 mL 50% sucrose solution via syringe into the mouth over 30 s 2 min prior to heel lance (n = 49)

    • 2 mL sterile water via syringe into the mouth over 30 s 2 min prior to heel lance (n = 47)

    In addition to the intervention to which the infants were assigned, parents were instructed to comfort the infant as much as possible.

    • Median crying time during heel lance, fraction of crying during sampling, crying time during first minute after end of sampling, total crying time

    • NIPS scores 1 min after heel lance and 1 min after blood sampling

    Median, 5th and 95th percentiles

    Median duration of first cry in group receiving 50% sucrose was lower (18 s (2 s to 75 s)) compared to placebo group (22 s (11 s to 143 s)) (P = 0.03). Median fraction of crying during sampling in 50% sucrose group was lower (43% (4% to 100%)) compared to placebo group (83% (20% to 100%)) (P = 0.004). Median crying time during first min after end of sampling in 50% sucrose group was lower (3 s (0 s to 58 s)) compared to placebo group (16 s (0 s to 59 s)) (P = 0.004). Median total time crying in 50% sucrose group was lower (30 s (2 s to 217 s)) compared to placebo group (71 s (13 s to 176 s)) (P = 0.007).

    No differences between groups with respect to changes in SpO2 (%) (P = 0.9)

    Median NIPS scores 1 min after heel lance were lower in 50% sucrose group compared to placebo group (3 (0 to 7) and 6 (0 to 7), respectively; P = 0.04). Median NIPS scores 1 min after end of blood sampling were lower in 50% sucrose group (0 (0 to 7)) compared to placebo group (2 (0 to 7)) (P = 0.05).

    As means were not reported, we could not include the results in RevMan analyses.

    Ramenghi 1999

    30 preterm infants, GA 32 weeks to 36 weeks, PNA > 24 h

    Heel lance

    • 25% sucrose solution (volume not reported) given via syringe into the mouth or via NG tube 2 min prior to first heel lance (n = 15), and via the alternate route for the second heel lance within 48 h

    • Sterile water given via syringe into the mouth or via NG tube 2 min prior to first heel lance and for the second heel lance the alternate route within 48 h (cross‐over design, n = 15)

    The cross‐over concerned mode of delivery of the solution: intraorally or via NG tube.

    Infants stayed in their original group assigned to receive either sucrose or sterile water.

    • Percentage of time crying over 5 min after sampling

    • Behavioural scores (4 facial expressions and the presence of cry) at 1 min, 3 min and 5 min after the lance for a total behavioural score

    Median, IQR

    Median percentage of time crying in intra‐oral water group was 22% (IQR 10.6% to 40%) and 27% (IQR 11.6% to 47%) for infants in NG tube water group. Median percentage of time crying in intra‐oral 25% sucrose group was 6% (IQR 0.6% to 15%) and 18.3% (IQR 11.6% to 41.6%) in NG tube 25% sucrose group.

    Reduction in crying time (P = 0.006) was noted in the 25% sucrose group compared with water group when infants received 25% sucrose intra‐orally, not via NG‐tube route. For infants in 25% sucrose group, a reduction in crying time was noted (P = 0.008) when solution was given intra‐orally compared to NG tube route.

    Behavioural scores for the intra‐oral water group were 9 (IQR 6 to 12) and 10 (IQR 6 to 14) for NG tube water group. Behavioural scores for intra‐oral 25% sucrose group were 5 (IQR 3 to 6) and 9 (IQR 8 to 10) for NG tube sucrose group. Reduction in behavioural scores was noted in 25% sucrose group (P = 0.002) compared with water group when infants received 25% sucrose intra‐orally but not via NG route. For infants in 25% sucrose group, there was a reduction in behavioural score (P = 0.001) when solution was given intra‐orally compared to via NG tube.

    Cross‐over design: data from the first assignment prior to cross‐over were presented. These data were not used in RevMan analyses.

    Stevens 2018

    245 neonates, 24 weeks' to 42 weeks' GA at birth and < 30 d of life or < 44 weeks' GA

    Heel lance

    All interventions given 2 min before heel lance

    • 0.1 mL 24% sucrose (n = 81)

    • 0.5 mL 24% sucrose (n = 81)

    • 1.0 mL 24% sucrose (n = 83)

    • PIPP‐R at 30 s and 60 s

    • Incidence of adverse events (HR > 240 beats/min or HR < 80 beats/min for > 20 s; SpO2 < 80% for > 20 s; no spontaneous respirations for > 20 s; and choking/gagging)

    Mean, SD

    Mean (SD) PIPP‐R scores at 30 s were 6.9 (3.5), 6.8 (3.2) and 6.7 (3.4) for 0.1 mL, 0.5 mL and 1.0 mL, respectively. There were no differences in PIPP‐R score between treatment groups at 30 s (P = 0.97) and at 60 s (P = 0.93).

    There were 5 adverse events reported: 3 infants experienced gagging, 1 had an HR < 80 beats/min and another developed 80% SpO2 following sucrose administration. There were no differences in proportion of adverse events between the intervention groups (P = 0.62), and all resolved spontaneously.
    No data could be used in RevMan analyses.

    Thakkar 2016

    Tutag Lehr 2015

    56 term infants ≤ 7 d old: appropriate for GA

    Heel lance

    • 2.0 mL 24% sucrose orally (n = 29)

    • Sterile water orally (n = 27)

    • NIPS

    • Mean SBF

    • SBF

    • PU measured by LDI during heel lance

    • HR

    • RR

    • SpO2 (%)

    • Adverse events

    Mean, SE, median, IQR

    Mean SBF and median NIPS scores immediately post‐heel lance were lower in sucrose‐treated infants (SBF: 167.9 PU ± 15.5 PU vs 205.4 PU ± 16.0 PU, P = 0.09; NIPS: 1 (IQR 0 to 4) vs 3 (IQR 0 to 6), P = 0.02), although there was no difference in mean SBF. During heel lance, NIPS score was predictive of SBF. An increase of 1 in NIPS score was associated with 11‐PU increase in SBF (R = 0.21; P = 0.09) for sucrose and 16‐PU increase for placebo‐treated infants (R = 0.20; P = 0.014).

    Increased SBF assessed by LDI is a pain response among term neonates following routine heel lance that was not completely attenuated by oral sucrose administration. Increased SBF is associated with NIPS scores. Sucrose analgesic efficacy evidenced by decreased NIPS scores for the sucrose group. Association of SBF with NIPS scores suggests LDI is potentially useful for assessing newborn procedural pain.

    SEs were transformed to SDs and were used in RevMan analyses for SBF.

    For HR, RR and SpO2, results were presented as means and SDs and were used in RevMan analyses.

    Assessed adverse events including

    gagging, choking and vomiting, and only 1 infant in the sucrose group was noted to experience a mild episode of 'spitting up'.

    Funding source: The Gerber Foundation, Fremont, Michigan

    Declaration of interest: The authors

    declared no conflict of interest.

    Velumula 2020

    94 neonates born between 30 1/7 weeks' and 36 6/7 weeks' GA

    Heel lance

    • 0.5 mL 24% sucrose (n = 47)

    • 2 mL expressed breast milk via syringe (n = 47)

    Infants in both groups also received swaddling 2 min prior to scheduled heel lance.

    • PIPP‐R scores at baseline; during the procedure; and at 30 s, 60 s, 90 s and 120 s postprocedure, assessed in real time by two investigators. Final pain score was determined by calculating the average of the two assessors' scores.

    • Adverse events

    • Mean, SD

    • Median, IQR

    PIPP‐R scores during procedure ranged from 5 to 12 in the two groups, with a mean (SD) score of 8.3 (1.3). PIPP‐R scores at 30 s and 60 s post‐procedure ranged from 0 to 6 and 0 to 5 with mean (SD) of 4.14 (0.98) and 1.27 (1.88) at 30 s and 60 s, respectively. All infants reached their baseline score of 0 by 90 s post‐procedure. PIPP‐R scores at 60 s were lower in the breast milk group compared to sucrose group. No differences were noted in PIPP‐R scores during the procedure (P = 0.17) and at 30 s post‐procedure (P = 0.39). PIPP‐R scores at 60 s post‐procedure were higher in the sucrose group compared to breast milk group (P < 0.01).

    No adverse events were noted in any infant in both groups.

    Funding source: Institutional grant from Sarnaik Endowment Grant, Detroit Medical Center

    Yilmaz 2010

    120 infants, GA 37 weeks to 42 weeks

    Mean GA (SD):

    • control group (n = 30): 39.67 weeks (0.80 weeks);

    • mother’s milk group (n = 30): 39.10 weeks (1.03 weeks);

    • sucrose group (n = 30): 39.10 weeks (0.71 weeks); and

    • pacifier group (n = 30): 39.20 weeks (0.93 weeks)

    Heel lance

    • Control group: infants lay in their mothers’ lap; no interventions were made before the painful procedure

    • Mother's milk group: 2 mL mother’s milk administered 2 min before the procedure by using an oral syringe and avoiding contact of the syringe with the mouth and lips

    • Sucrose group: 2 mL 20% sucrose via a syringe as above, 2 min before the procedure

    • Pacifier group: given a pacifier

    • NIPS score

    • HR

    • RR

    • Crying time

    Mean, SD

    No differences in HR and SpO2 between groups

    After the procedure, the mean crying time of the sucrose group was shorter than those of the other groups. Comparing the crying times of the control and experimental groups according to the procedure time showed no differences between the values before and during the procedure (F = 1.50, P > 0.05); (F = 2.43, P > 0.05).

    Before the procedure, the lowest NIPS mean was in the sucrose group, and the highest NIPS mean was in the pacifier group. During the procedure, no differences were found between the groups for NIPS means (P > 0.05). After the procedure, the sucrose group showed the lowest response to pain, while the mother's milk group had the highest response. Comparing the NIPS means of the control and experimental groups according to the procedure times, differences were found between the groups for values obtained before and after the procedure (F = 3.49, P < 0.05); (F = 6.71, P < 0.05).

    Outcome data were presented according to different procedure times, and no data could be included in RevMan analyses.

    Ogawa 2005

    100 healthy full‐term infants:

    • Heel lance + water group: GA 40 weeks (range 38 weeks to 42 weeks)

    • Heel lance + sucrose group: GA 39 weeks (range 37 weeks to 41 weeks)

    • Venipuncture + water group: GA 39 weeks (range 37 weeks to 41 weeks)

    • Venipuncture + sucrose group: GA 39 weeks (range 37 weeks to 41 weeks)

    Heel lance or venipuncture

    2 min before procedure:

    • heel lance + 0.1 mL sterile water on infant's tongue via syringe (n = 25);

    • heel lance + 0.1 mL 50% sucrose on infant's tongue via syringe (n = 25);

    • venipuncture + 0.1 mL sterile water on infant's tongue via syringe (n = 25); and

    • venipuncture + 0.1 mL 50% sucrose on infant's tongue via syringe (n = 25).

    Duration of first cry (s), first crying time/total procedure time (%) and the ratio of crying to no crying

    NFCS score:

    • 1 min after oral administration of water or sucrose;

    • disinfection of skin before heel lance or venipuncture;

    • during skin puncture;

    • during blood sampling;

    • during compression to stop bleeding;

    • during application of plaster; and

    • 1 min after application of plaster.

    Reported medians, range and mean, SD

    Reported in graph form, median and IQR

    Reduction in duration of first cry in heel lance group given sucrose compared to heel lance alone (P < 0.001)

    Reduced NFCS scores in sucrose group during heel lance (median 47, IQR 31 to 60) and during compression to stop bleeding (median 32, IQR 8 to 54) compared to the water group (median 58, IQR 54 to 65; median 52, IQR 41 to 61, respectively) (P < 0.001)

    Sucrose did not reduce NFCS scores during or after venipuncture

    We included data for sucrose vs water for heel lance and for venipuncture for duration of first cry.

    Funding source: not specified

    Competing interests: none declared

    GA: gestational age; IQR: interquartile range; NFCS: Neonatal Facial Coding System; SD: standard deviation
