    Gaspardo 2008

    33 preterm infants, median PMA 30 weeks

    Venipuncture, arterial puncture, heel lance, intravenous cannulation, endotracheal tube introduction, endotracheal tube suctioning, gavage insertion for feeding, removal of electrode leads and tape

    On day 1, no treatment was given to any neonate in order to collect baseline data. After that, on days 2 to 4 before every minor painful procedure, infants received either:

    • sucrose (n = 17): 0.5 mL/kg 25% sucrose; or

    • water (n = 16): 0.5 mL/kg sterile water.

    Pain was assessed over 4 d during morning blood collection (heel lance).

    Incidence of cry (percentage of neonates crying), HR (percentage of neonates with HR ≥ 160 beats/min),

    NFCS (percentage of neonates with score ≥ 3), Activated Behavioural State (percentage of neonates with score ≥ 4). The assessment was divided into 5 phases: baseline antisepsis, puncture, dressing and recovery. The neonates’
    facial activity (NFCS), behavioural state and HR were evaluated.

    No means or SDs reported;

    NFCS results reported in graph form only

    The data analysis used cutoff scores for painful and distressful responses. There were fewer sucrose‐group neonates with facial actions signalling pain than water‐group neonates in puncture phase and in antisepsis phase. There were fewer sucrose‐group neonates crying during antisepsis phase, puncture phase and dressing phase. There was no difference between groups for physiological response. The efficacy of sucrose was maintained for pain relief in preterm neonates, with no side effects.

    Data could not be used in RevMan analyses.

    No side effects of using sucrose were detected.

    Funding source: This study was supported by the FAPESP,

    the CNPq, and the FAEPA

    Declaration of interest: not specified

    Johnston 2002

    103 infants:

    • sucrose group: mean PMA 28.18 weeks (1.72 weeks); and

    • water group: mean PMA 28.05 weeks (2.06 weeks).

    Every time the infant was to undergo an invasive (e.g. heel lance, intravenous cannulation, arterial puncture, injection) or noninvasive but presumably uncomfortable procedure (e.g. endotracheal tube suctioning, tape or
    lead removal, gavage insertion for feeding), the infant received sucrose or water.

    • Sucrose group (n = 51): 0.1 mL 24% sucrose in sterile refrigerated syringe

    • Water group (n = 52): 0.1 mL water in sterile refrigerated syringe

    The solution was in a syringe and administered into the infant’s mouth at the beginning of the procedure, 2 min into the procedure and another 2 min into the procedure. If the procedure was > 15 min, up to another 3 doses (0.1 mL each) were to be given 2 min apart.

    Neurobehavioural development was assessed by the subscales of alertness and orientation and motor development and vigour of the NAPI, SNAP and NBRS.

    SNAP was measured for each 24‐hour period during the study week, and NBRS was measured at 2 weeks’ PNA and at discharge.

    Beta, CI (multiple regression)

    On the basis of analysis of covariance with PMA at birth and the number of invasive procedures as covariates, there were no group differences (between sucrose and water) for any of the secondary outcomes of NBRS at 2 weeks' PNA (P = 0.426) or at discharge (P = 0.965). In the sucrose group only, a higher number of sucrose doses predicted lower scores on motor development and vigour and on alertness and orientation at 36 weeks' PNA, lower motor development and vigour at 40 weeks' PNA, and higher NBRS at 2 weeks' PNA. A higher number of invasive procedures was predictive of higher NBRS both times in the water group.

    No differences were found between the sucrose and water groups for NAPI.

    Data could not be used in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: Fonds de la Recherche en

    Sante du Quebec (961297‐104), the IWK Foundation, and Health

    Declaration of interest: not specified

    Taddio 2008

    240 newborn infants born to nondiabetic and diabetic mothers, PMA ≥ 36 weeks

    3 heel lances, venipuncture and IM vitamin K injection; multiple painful stimuli

    • 2 mL 24% sucrose given to infants of nondiabetic mothers (n = 60)

    • 2 mL 24% sucrose given to infants of diabetic mothers (n = 60)

    • 2 mL sterile water given to infants of nondiabetic mothers (n = 60)

    • 2 mL sterile water given to infants of diabetic mothers (n = 60)

    PIPP scores overall, during IM injection, during venipuncture and during all 3 heel lances

    Safety

    Mean, SD, 95% CI

    Overall PIPP scores were lower among newborns given sucrose (mean 6.8, SD 2.9) compared to water (mean 8.1, SD 2.5) (MD −1.3, 95% CI −2.0 to −0.6; P < 0.001).

    PIPP scores during IM injection did not differ between the sucrose and water group for nondiabetic (P = 0.10) or diabetic mothers (P = 0.15).

    PIPP scores during venipuncture were lower among infants of nondiabetic mothers who received sucrose compared to water (mean score 5.7, 95% CI 4.7 to 6.7 vs mean score 8.9, 95% CI 7.9 to 9.9; P < 0.001). Similar results were found amongst infants of diabetic mothers (sucrose: mean score 6.8, 95% CI 5.7 to 7.9 vs water: mean score 9.2, 95% CI 8.4 to 10.1; P < 0.001).

    During the first 3 heel lances, newborns from diabetic mothers receiving sucrose or water did not have different PIPP scores.

    Results for different painful stimuli were reported separately.

    Data could be used in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: CIHR grant (MCT 63143) and a Sick Kids Foundation

    graduate student Research Training Competition Award

    Competing interests: none declared

    CI: confidence interval; CIHR: Canadian Institutes of Health Research; CNPq: National Council of Science and Technology Development; FAEPA: Foundation for Support of Teaching, Research and Assistance of the University Hospital of Clinics; FAPESP: Foundation for Support of Research in the State of São Paulo, Brazil; HR: heart rate; IM: intramuscular; MD: mean difference; NAPI: Neurobehavioral Assessment of the Preterm Infant; NBRS: Neuro‐Biological Risk Score; NFCS: Neonatal Facial Coding System; PIPP: Premature Infant Pain Profile; PMA: postmenstrual age; PNA: postnatal age; SD: standard deviation; SNAP: Score for Neonatal Acute Physiology

    If authors reported data in such a way that we could not include the results in RevMan analyses, we reported the findings according to the authors in tables (Table 1, Table 2, Table 3). The results of 32 studies could not be included in RevMan analyses (Altun‐Köroğlu 2010; Benoit 2021; Blass 1997; Blass 1999; Bucher 1995; Campbell‐Yeo 2019; Codipietro 2008; Gaspardo 2008; Gormally 2001; Johnston 1997; Johnston 2002; Lago 2020; Leng 2013; Leng 2015; Liaw 2013; Marin Gabriel 2013; McGinnis 2016; Mitchell 2016; Nimbalkar 2020; Okan 2007; Ors 1999; Overgaard 1999; Ramenghi 1996b; Ramenghi 1996a; Ramenghi 1999; Rushforth 1993; Stevens 2005a; Stevens 2018; Storm 2002; Thakkar 2016; Velumula 2020; Yilmaz 2010). The results of those studies are summarised after the analyses for each comparison.

    No studies assessing long‐term neurodevelopmental outcomes were identified.

    The studies were conducted in 16 different countries; Canada (11), US (9), Turkey (7), UK (6), India (4), Italy (3) China (3). Two studies were conducted in Australia, Spain, and Switzerland, and one study was conducted in Brazil, Denmark, Japan, Netherlands, Norway, and Taiwan.

    Painful procedures

    Heel lance was exclusively studied in 50 trials (Abbasoglu 2015; Altun‐Köroğlu 2010; Asmerom 2013; Benoit 2021; Blass 1997; Blass 1999; Bucher 1995; Chang 2020; Cignacco 2012; Codipietro 2008; Gao 2018; Gibbins 2002; Gormally 2001; Greenberg 2002; Guala 2001; Haouari 1995; Harrison 2003; Isik 2000a; Johnston 1997; Johnston 1999; Kumari 2017; Lago 2020; Leng 2013; Leng 2015; Liaw 2013; Marin Gabriel 2013; Mathai 2006; McGinnis 2016; Mitchell 2016; Nimbalkar 2020; Okan 2007; Overgaard 1999; Ramenghi 1996b; Ramenghi 1996a; Ramenghi 1999; Rushforth 1993; Sen 2020; Shah 2017; Simonse 2012; Slater 2010; Stevens 1999; Stevens 2005a; Stevens 2018; Storm 2002; Thakkar 2016; Tutag Lehr 2015; Unceta‐Barranechea 2008; Velumula 2020; Yilmaz 2010; Ors 1999) (Table 1). One study assessed both heel lance and venipuncture (Ogawa 2005) (Table 2). Three studies assessed multiple painful procedures (Gaspardo 2008; Johnston 2002; Taddio 2008) (Table 3). One study provided the interventions for all painful procedures during the entire hospital stay of infants; however, outcomes were assessed during three medically indicated heel lances (Campbell‐Yeo 2019).

    Twenty‐two studies evaluated adverse effects.

    Ongoing studies

    We identified four ongoing studies (three studies focusing on preterm infants and one focusing on term infants) in clinical trial registry platforms. (CTRI/2012/06/002730; NCT01742520; NCT01894659; NCT02109263); two studies will compare breast milk to sucrose; one study will compare sucrose to breastfeeding; and one study will compare sucrose to glucose (See Characteristics of ongoing studies).

    Studies awaiting classification

    We listed two studies as awaiting classification as we have not been able to locate full‐text copies in libraries in either Canada or the USA (Aldás 2007; Singh 2001) (See Characteristics of studies awaiting classification).

    Excluded studies

    We excluded 57 studies after full‐text review. Reasons for exclusion are described for each study in the Characteristics of excluded studies table.

    Risk of bias in included studies

    The included studies enrolled between 15 (Ramenghi 1996b), and 671 infants (Leng 2015). Thirty‐one (56.3%) enrolled more than 100 infants. The largest study enrolled 671 term infants, who were randomised into four groups (Leng 2015); sucrose, sucrose + NNS, sucrose + swaddling, and sucrose + NNS + swaddling. The second‐largest study enrolled 560 infants (Leng 2013), but we could not include data for sucrose versus sterile water for increase in heart rate and decrease in oxygen saturation at three minutes after heel lance in RevMan analyses, as they were reported as means and full ranges.

    Few researchers provided a definition of pain or how it was conceptualised in relation to the outcomes. There were differences in study methods. Heel lance was studied as the pain stimulus, however, little detail about this procedure (e.g. manual versus automated lance) was provided. Therefore, it is impossible to know if the painful stimuli were comparable in intensity, duration or frequency across studies. The length of infant observation following the heel lance was infrequently reported, and may have implications for the incidence of reported adverse effects.

    The delivery method of sucrose differed between studies (syringe, dropper, sucrose‐dipped pacifier), as did the concentrations of sucrose and the volume administered. Outcomes were reported inconsistently; as means with SDs, standard errors (SE) or 95% CIs, or medians with ranges and often in graphic form without reporting of numerical data.

    The risk of bias is reported in the Risk of bias graph (Figure 2), and the Risk of bias summary (Figure 3).

    • Open in figure viewer
    Figure 2

    Risk of bias graph: review authors' judgements about each risk of bias item presented as percentages across all included studies

    • Open in figure viewer
    Figure 3

    Risk of bias summary: review authors' judgements about each risk of bias item for each included study

    Allocation

    Thirty‐five studies (63.6%) reported an adequate random sequence generation (selection bias), however, in 20 studies (36.3%), it was unclear how the random sequence was generated. No study had a high risk of bias.

    In 24 studies (43.6%), allocation concealment (selection bias) was adequate. There was a high risk of bias for allocation concealment in 13 studies (23.6%), and an unclear risk of bias in 18 studies (32.7%).

    Blinding

    There was a low risk of bias in the blinding of participants and personnel (performance and detection bias) in 25 studies (45.4%), and a high risk detected in 20 studies (36.3%). There was an unclear risk of bias in 10 studies (18.2%).

    There was a low risk of bias in the blinding of outcome assessments (detection bias) in 34 studies (61.8%), high risk in 12 studies (21.8%), and an unclear risk in nine studies (16.3%).

    Incomplete outcome data

    There was a low risk of attrition bias in 44 studies (80%), a high risk in four studies (7.3%), and an unclear risk in seven studies (12.7%).

    Selective reporting

    Thirteen studies (23.6%) had a low risk of reporting bias. One study (1.8%) had a high risk of reporting bias and the risk of reporting bias was unclear for 41 studies (74.5%).

    Other potential sources of bias

    The risk of bias was unclear in two studies (3.7%), and we did not detect other sources of bias in 53 studies (96.3%).

    Effects of interventions

    See: Summary of findings 1 Summary of findings table ‐ Sucrose (any concentration, any dose, with or without NNS) compared to control (water, placebo, or no intervention) for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates; Summary of findings 2 Summary of findings table ‐ Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to NNS for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates; Summary of findings 3 Summary of findings table ‐ Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) + NNS compared to NNS (with or without water) for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates; Summary of findings 4 Summary of findings table ‐ Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to glucose (any concentration, any dose) for heel lancing in neonates; Summary of findings 5 Summary of findings table ‐ Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to breastfeeding for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates; Summary of findings 6 Summary of findings table ‐ Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to expressed breast milk for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates; Summary of findings 7 Summary of findings table ‐ Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to laser acupuncture for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates; Summary of findings 8 Summary of findings table ‐ Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to facilitated tucking for analgesia during repeated heel lancing in neonates; Summary of findings 9 Summary of findings table ‐ Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to skin to skin care + water for analgesia during repeated heel lancing in neonates

    Comparison 1. Sucrose (any concentration, any dose, with or without NNS) compared to control (water, placebo, or no intervention) for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates

    summary of findings Table 1

    Primary outcome
    PIPP scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing (term and preterm infants)

    Seven studies reported this outcome for a total of 547 term and preterm infants (Asmerom 2013; Gao 2018; Gibbins 2002; Mitchell 2016; Slater 2010; Stevens 1999; Taddio 2008). The study with the smallest sample size reported on 42 preterm infants (Gao 2018), and the largest study reported on 128 term and preterm infants (Gibbins 2002). The evidence suggests that sucrose (20‐30%, 0.1‐2 mL), with or without NNS, probably results in a reduction in PIPP scores compared to the control group at 30 seconds after lancing (MD ‐1.74 (95% CI ‐2.11 to ‐1.37); 7 studies, 547 infants; I2 = 62%; moderate‐certainty evidence: Analysis 1.1; Figure 4).

    Three (Mitchell 2016; Slater 2010; Taddio 2008) of the seven studies reported this outcome for a total of 277 term infants. The evidence suggests that sucrose results in little to no difference in PIPP scores compared to control at 30 seconds after heel lancing (MD ‐0.87 (95% CI ‐1.80 to 0.06); 3 studies, 277 infants; I2 = 47%; low‐certainty evidence: Analysis 1.2; Figure 4).

    Three (Asmerom 2013; Gao 2018; Stevens 1999) of the seven studies reported this outcome for a total of 192 preterm infants. The evidence suggests that sucrose results in little to no difference in PIPP scores compared to control at 30 seconds after heel lancing (MD ‐1.88 (95% CI ‐2.32 to ‐1.44); 3 studies, 192 infants; I2 = 75%; low‐certainty evidence: Analysis 1.3; Figure 4).

    PIPP scores at 60 seconds after heel lancing (term and preterm infants)

    Two studies reported this outcome for a total of 164 infants. One study reported this outcome for 119 term and preterm infants (Gibbins 2002). Another study reported on 45 preterm infants, who received either pacifier dipped in sucrose or pacifier dipped in water (Stevens 1999). The evidence suggests that sucrose probably results in a reduction in PIPP scores compared with water at 60 seconds after heel lancing (MD ‐2.14 (95% CI ‐3.34 to ‐0.94); 2 studies, 164 infants; I2 = 0%; moderate‐certainty evidence: Analysis 1.4; Figure 4).

    DAN scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing (term infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 32 term neonates (Mathai 2006). The evidence is very uncertain about the effects of 20% sucrose on DAN scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing compared to water (MD ‐1.90 (95% CI ‐8.58 to 4.78); 1 study, 32 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 1.5).

    NIPS scores immediately after heel lancing (term infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 56 term infants (Tutag Lehr 2015). The evidence suggests that sucrose (24%) probably results in a reduction in NIPS scores immediately after heel lancing compared to the water group (MD ‐2.00 (95% CI ‐2.42 to ‐1.58); 1 study, 56 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; moderate‐certainty evidence; Analysis 1.6).

    Secondary outcomes
    Total crying time (in seconds) after lancing

    Four studies reported this outcome for a total of 222 term infants (Chang 2020; Greenberg 2002; Isik 2000a; Mathai 2006). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on total crying time (in seconds) in the sucrose group (12‐30%, 0.33‐2 mL) compared to the control group after lancing (MD ‐30.74 (95% CI ‐40.73 to ‐20.74); 4 studies, 222 infants; I2 = 96%; Analysis 1.7).

    Duration of first cry (in seconds) after lancing

    Three studies (Haouari 1995; Harrison 2003; Ogawa 2005) reported this outcome for 190 infants. The evidence suggests that sucrose may result in a reduction in the duration of the first cry (measured from the start of the vocalisation until a quiet interval of 5 seconds) after heel lancing compared to the control group (MD ‐53.18 (95% CI ‐76.90 to ‐39.46); 3 studies, 190 infants; I2 = 69%; Analysis 1.8). One study (Mathai 2006) reported on 32 infants; the evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose compared to the control group (MD ‐5.00 (95% CI ‐17.40 to ‐7.40); 1 study, 32 infants, heterogeneity not applicable) for the duration of first cry (measured from the start of the vocalisation until before first inspiration).

    Heart rate (bpm) during heel lancing

    Two studies reporting this outcome with term infants were included (Guala 2001;Tutag Lehr 2015). For one study, data from four different groups were included in the analyses (Guala 2001). The evidence suggests that sucrose results in little to no difference in heart rate during heel lance compared to control groups (MD 2.21 (95% CI ‐3.95 to 8.37); 2 studies, 136 infants; I2 = 0; Analysis 1.9).

    Percentage change in heart rate after heel lancing

    Two studies reported this outcome for 86 term infants (Haouari 1995; Isik 2000a). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on the percentage of change in heart rate one minute after heel lance compared with the control group (MD 0.90 (95% CI ‐5.81 to 7.61); 2 studies, 86 infants; I2 = 86%; Analysis 1.10).

    Respiratory rate (rpm) during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 56 term infants (Tutag Lehr 2015). The evidence suggests that sucrose results in little to no difference in the respiratory rate (breaths/minute) during heel lancing compared to the control group (MD ‐1.00 (95% CI ‐7.64 to 5.64); 1 study, 56 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 1.11).

    Oxygen saturation (%) during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 56 term infants (Tutag Lehr 2015). The evidence suggests that sucrose results in little to no difference in the oxygen saturation (%) during heel lancing compared to the control group (MD ‐5.00 (95% CI ‐12.79 to 2.79); 1 study, 56 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 1.12).

    Skin blood flow (PU) during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 56 term infants (Tutag Lehr 2015). The evidence suggests that sucrose results in little to no difference in skin blood flow (PU) during heel lance compared to the control group (MD ‐32.00 (95% CI ‐68.87 to 4.87); 1 study, 56 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 1.13).

    Nociceptive‐specific brain activity

    One study reported this outcome for 44 term infants (Slater 2010). The evidence suggests that sucrose results in little to no difference in the nociceptive‐specific brain activity (mean weight by EEG) compared to the water group (MD 0.02 (95% CI ‐0.05 to 0.09); 1 study, 44 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 1.14).

    Comparison 2. Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to NNS for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates

    summary of findings Table 2

    Primary outcome
    PIPP scores during recovery after heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 43 preterm infants (Gao 2018). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of 20% sucrose compared to NNS on PIPP scores during the recovery period after heel lancing (MD 0.60 (95% CI ‐0.30 to 1.50); 1 study, 43 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 2.1).

    DAN scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing (term infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 37 term infants (Mathai 2006). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of 20% sucrose compared to NNS on DAN scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing (MD ‐1.20 (95% CI ‐7.87 to 5.47); 1 study, 37 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 2.2).

    Secondary outcomes
    Percentage of crying time during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 43 preterm infants (Gao 2018). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on the percentage of crying time during the heel lance compared to the NNS group (MD 9.60 (95% CI 1.41 to 17.79); 1 study, 43 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 2.3).

    Total crying time (in seconds) after lancing

    One study reported on 37 term infants (Mathai 2006). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on total crying time after lancing compared to the NNS group (MD 36.00 (95% CI 27.00 to 45.00); 1 study, 37 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 2.4).

    Duration of first cry (in seconds) after lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 37 term infants (Mathai 2006). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on the duration of first cry compared to the NNS group (MD ‐2.00 (95% CI ‐8.78 to 4.78); 1 study, 37 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 2.5).

    Heart rate (bpm) during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 43 preterm infants (Gao 2018). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on heart rate (beats/min) during the heel lance compared to the NNS group (MD ‐2.60 (95% CI ‐8.17 to 2.97); 1 study, 43 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 2.6).

    Oxygen saturation (%) during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 43 preterm infants (Gao 2018). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on oxygen saturation (%) during the heel lance compared to NNS group (MD 0.60 (95% CI ‐0.64 to 1.84); 1 study, 43 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 2.7).

    Comparison 3. Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) + NNS compared to NNS (with or without water) for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates

    summary of findings Table 3

    Primary outcome
    PIPP scores during heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 44 preterm infants (Gao 2018). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of 20% sucrose + NNS on PIPP scores during heel lance in infants compared to the NNS group (MD ‐4.90 (95% CI ‐5.73 to ‐4.07); 1 study, 44 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 3.1).

    PIPP scores during recovery after heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 44 preterm infants (Gao 2018). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of 20% sucrose + NNS on PIPP scores during recovery after heel lancing compared to the NNS group (MD ‐3.80 (95% CI ‐4.47 to ‐3.13); 1 study, 44 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 3.2).

    NFCS during heel lancing (term infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 100 term infants (Unceta‐Barranechea 2008). The evidence is very uncertain about the effects of 24% sucrose + NNS on NFCS scores during heel lancing compared to NNS + water (MD ‐0.60 (95% CI ‐1.47 to 0.27); 1 study, 100 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 3.3).

    Secondary outcomes
    Crying time (%) during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 44 preterm infants (Gao 2018). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose + NNS lower crying time (%) during the heel lance compared to the NNS group (MD ‐32.70 (95% CI ‐38.37 to ‐27.03); 1 study, 44 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 3.4).

    Percentage of crying time (in seconds) after heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 100 term infants and compared sucrose + NNS with water + NNS (Unceta‐Barranechea 2008). Another study reported on 42 term infants and compared a sucrose‐coated pacifier with a water‐moistened pacifier (Greenberg 2002). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose + NNS on crying time (in seconds) compared to the water + NNS group (MD ‐1.41 s (95% CI ‐9.87 to 7.04); 2 studies, 142 infants; I2 = 83%; Analysis 3.5).

    Heart rate (bpm) during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 44 preterm infants (Gao 2018). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose + NNS on heart rate (beats/min) during the heel lance compared to the NNS group (MD ‐15.60 (95% CI ‐20.60 to 18.27; 1 study, 44 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 3.6).

    Oxygen saturation (%) during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 44 preterm infants (Gao 2018). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose + NNS on oxygen saturation (%) during the heel lance compared to the NNS group (MD 2.30 (95% CI 1.09 to 3.51); 1 study, 44 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 3.7).

    Comparison 4. Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to glucose (any concentration, any dose) for heel lancing in neonates

    summary of findings Table 4

    Primary outcome
    PIPP scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 94 preterm infants (Kumari 2017). The evidence suggests that 24% sucrose results in little to no difference in PIPP scores 30 seconds after heel lancing compared to 25% glucose (MD 0.26 (95% CI ‐0.70 to 1.22); 1 study, 94 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 4.1).

    PIPP scores at 60 seconds after heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 94 preterm infants (Kumari 2017). The evidence suggests that 24% sucrose results in little to no difference in PIPP scores 60 seconds after heel lancing compared to 25% glucose (MD ‐0.02 (95% CI ‐0.79 to 0.75); 1 study, 94 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 4.2).

    Secondary outcomes
    Heart rate (bpm) during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for four different groups comparing sucrose 33%, sucrose 50%, glucose 33%, and glucose 50% (Guala 2001). The evidence suggests glucose results in a slight reduction in the heart rate (beats/minute) during heel lance compared with the sucrose group (MD 10.51 (95% CI 1.12 to 19.89); 1 study, 80 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 4.3).

    Percentage change in heart rate 60 seconds after heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 56 term infants (Isik 2000a). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose in the percentage change in heart rate one minute after heel lance compared to the glucose group (MD ‐6.58 (95% CI ‐14.85 to 1.69); 1 study, 56 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 4.4).

    Total crying time (in seconds) after heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 56 term infants (Isik 2000a). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose in total crying time (in seconds) after heel lance compared to the glucose group (MD ‐34.89 (95% CI ‐61.67 to ‐8.11); 1 study, 56 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 4.5).

    Comparison 5. Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to breastfeeding for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates

    summary of findings Table 5

    Primary outcome
    PIPP scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing (term and preterm infants)

    Two studies reported this outcome for a total of 148 term and preterm infants (Codipietro 2008; Simonse 2012). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of 24‐25% sucrose (1‐2 mL), with or without NNS, compared to breastfeeding, on PIPP scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing (MD ‐0.70 (95% CI ‐0.49 to 1.88); 2 studies, 148 infants; I2 = 94 %; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 5.1; Figure 8).

    COMFORTneo scores after heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 47 preterm infants (Simonse 2012). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of 24% sucrose on COMFORTneo scores after heel lancing compared to breastfeeding (MD ‐2.60 (95% CI ‐3.06 to ‐2.14); 1 study, 47 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 5.2).

    Comparison 6. Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to expressed breast milk for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates

    summary of findings Table 6

    Primary outcome
    PIPP‐R scores during heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 88 preterm infants (Velumula 2020). The evidence suggests that 24% sucrose may result in little to no difference in PIPP‐R scores during heel lancing compared to expressed breast milk (MD 0.3 (95% CI ‐0.24 to 0.84); 1 study, 88 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 6.1).

    PIPP‐R scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 94 preterm infants (Velumula 2020). The evidence suggests that 24% sucrose may result in little to no difference in PIPP‐R scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing compared to expressed breast milk (MD 0.30 (95% CI ‐0.11 to 0.71); 1 study, 88 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 6.2).

    PIPP‐R scores at 60 seconds after heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 94 preterm infants (Velumula 2020). The evidence suggests that 24% sucrose probably may result in slightly increased PIPP‐R scores at 60 seconds after heel lancing compared to expressed breast milk (MD 1.10 (95% CI 0.34 to 1.86); 1 study, 88 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 6.3).

    DAN scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing (term infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 35 term infants (Mathai 2006). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of 20% sucrose on DAN scores at 30 seconds after lancing compared to expressed breast milk (MD ‐1.80 (95% CI ‐8.47 to 4.87); 1 study, 35 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 6.4).

    Secondary outcomes
    Total crying time (in seconds) after heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 35 term infants (Mathai 2006). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on crying time after heel lancing compared to the human milk group in this study (MD ‐8.00 s (95% CI ‐21.07 to 5.07); 1 study, 35 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 6.5).

    Comparison 7. Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to recorded music for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates

    Primary outcome
    PIPP‐R scores at 1 minute after heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 75 preterm infants (Shah 2017). There was no difference in PIPP‐R scores between the 24% sucrose and music groups; however, the data were reported as median and interquartile range and were not included in the analysis.

    Secondary outcomes
    Heart rate (bpm) during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 35 preterm and term infants (Shah 2017). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on heart rate (beats/min) during heel lancing compared to the recorded music group (MD 8.00 (95% CI ‐0.43 to 16.43); 1 study, 35 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 7.1).

    Oxygen saturation (%) during heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 35 preterm and term infants (Shah 2017). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on oxygen saturation (%) during heel lancing compared to the recorded music group (MD ‐0.20 (95% CI ‐1.36 to 0.96); 1 study, 35 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 7.2).

    Comparison 8. Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to laser acupuncture for analgesia for heel lancing in neonates

    summary of findings Table 7

    Primary outcome
    NIPS scores during heel lancing (term infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 42 term infants (Abbasoglu 2015). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of 24% sucrose compared to laser acupuncture on NIPS scores during heel lance (MD ‐0.86 (95% CI ‐1.43 to ‐0.29); 1 study, 42 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 8.1).

    Secondary outcomes
    Total crying time (in seconds) after heel lancing

    One study reported this outcome for 42 term infants (Abbasoglu 2015). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose on total crying time after heel lancing compared to the laser acupuncture group (MD ‐51.29 (95% CI ‐73.11 to ‐29.47); 1 study, 42 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; Analysis 8.2).

    Comparison 9. Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to facilitated tucking for analgesia during repeated heel lancing in neonates

    summary of findings Table 8

    Primary outcome
    BPSN scores during heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 48 preterm infants (Cignacco 2012). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of 20% sucrose in total BPSN scores during heel lancing compared to facilitated tucking (MD ‐2.27 (95% CI ‐4.66 to 0.12); heterogeneity not applicable; 1 study, 48 infants; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 9.1).

    BPSN scores during recovery after heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study reported this outcome for 48 preterm infants (Cignacco 2012). The evidence is very uncertain about the effect of 20% sucrose in total BPSN scores during recovery after heel lancing compared to facilitated tucking (MD ‐0.31 (95% CI ‐1.72 to 1.10); 1 study, 48 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; very low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 9.2).

    Comparison 10. Sucrose (any concentration, any dose) compared to skin‐to‐skin care + water for analgesia during repeated heel lancing in neonates

    summary of findings Table 9

    Primary outcome
    PIPP scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study compared this outcome on the effects of 24% sucrose compared to skin‐to‐skin care on PIPP scores after repeated heel lances (Campbell‐Yeo 2019). The evidence suggests that sucrose compared to skin‐to‐skin plus water results in little to no difference in PIPP scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing #1 (MD 0.13 (95% CI ‐0.70 to 0.96); 1 study, 151 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence), heel lancing #2 (MD ‐0.56 (95% CI ‐1.57 to 0.45); 1 study, 144 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence), or heel lancing #3 (MD‐0.15 (95% CI ‐1.26 to 0.96); 1 study, 111 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence; Analysis 10.1).

    PIPP scores at 60 seconds after heel lancing (preterm infants)

    One study compared this outcome on the effects of 24% sucrose compared to skin‐to‐skin care on PIPP scores after repeated heel lances (Campbell‐Yeo 2019). The evidence suggests that sucrose compared to skin‐to‐skin plus water results in little to no difference in PIPP scores at 60 seconds after heel lancing #1 (MD –0.61 (95% CI ‐1.55 to 0.33); 1 study, 154 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence), heel lancing #2 (MD ‐0.12 (95% CI ‐0.99 to 0.75); 1 study, 143 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence), or heel lancing #3 (MD‐0.40 (95% CI ‐1.48 to 0.68); 1 study, 111 infants; heterogeneity not applicable; low‐certainty evidence); Analysis 10.2).

    Data from 30 studies could not be included in analyses in RevMan (Altun‐Köroğlu 2010; Benoit 2021; Blass 1997; Blass 1999; Bucher 1995; Gaspardo 2008; Gormally 2001; Johnston 1997; Johnston 1999; Johnston 2002; Lago 2020; Leng 2013; Leng 2015; Liaw 2013; Marin Gabriel 2013; McGinnis 2016; Nimbalkar 2020; Okan 2007; Ors 1999; Overgaard 1999; Ramenghi 1996b; Ramenghi 1996a; Ramenghi 1999; Rushforth 1993; Sen 2020; Stevens 2005a; Stevens 2018; Storm 2002; Thakkar 2016; Yilmaz 2010); for details see Table 1, though a brief summary is provided below.

    In the Altun‐Köroğlu 2010 study, there were no differences observed between the hind milk group and the sucrose group for all measures. In a study comparing the influence of breastfeeding or oral sucrose on pain related electrophysiologic activity, pain scores, and physiologic recovery (Benoit 2021), both interventions reduced bio‐behavioural responses to pain; however, the interventions may differentially modulate pain response in the infant brain. In Blass 1997, there was less crying time during blood collection in the sucrose group than in the water group and, in the later Blass 1999 study, sucrose diminished cry duration compared to water. In Bucher 1995, cry duration was reduced by sucrose compared to water. Gormally 2001 reported no difference in facial activity related to pain between infants who received sucrose and those who did not. Johnston 1997 found a decrease in percentage facial action in the sucrose alone group and the sucrose plus rocking group compared to the water group. Lago 2020 investigated whether a repeated dose of 24% sucrose associated with NNS and facilitated tucking was more effective than a single dose of 24% (with volumes varying from 0.3 to 0.5 mL according to infant's weight). There were no differences in pain scores between the two groups. In the large study, Leng 2013, the average pain score three minutes after the procedure was lower in the sucrose groups compared to the glucose groups. Liaw 2013 found that infants receiving NNS + oral sucrose + tucking or NNS + oral sucrose experienced more quiet sleep occurrences than those receiving routine care. Marin Gabriel 2013 reported lower percentage of crying with both the breastfed combined with skin‐to‐skin contact group and also the sucrose plus skin‐to‐skin contact group compared with the skin‐to‐skin contact group. McGinnis 2016 described lower NPASS scores over time measured at baseline, immediately after the heel lance, and two and five minutes after heel lance for the 24% sucrose (0.1 to 0.2 mL) + NNS + swaddling + vibration group compared to the 24% sucrose (0.1 to 0.2 mL) + NNS + swaddling group. The effects of 0.5 mL of 24% sucrose compared to skin‐to‐skin care on PIPP scores during heel lances in a cross‐over study by Nimbalkar 2020 indicated no differences between the groups. In Okan 2007, the sucrose and glucose groups showed a shorter time for the duration of first cry and total crying time than the water group. Ors 1999 noted a decrease in crying time for the sucrose group compared to the human milk or sterile water groups, and recovery time from crying was shorter in the sucrose group, as was the percentage change in heart rate after heel lance. In Overgaard 1999, the NIPS scores one minute after heel lance and one minute after blood sampling were lower in the sucrose group than the water group. Ramenghi 1996b noted lower mean pain scores in the group receiving sucrose at one and two minutes after heel lance compared to the water group. In Ramenghi 1996a, the pain scores were lower in the sucrose (25% and 50%) groups and the Calpol group compared to the water group. In the third study by Ramenghi 1999, which was a cross‐over design (sucrose and water were given by nasogastric tube or intra‐orally, but the infant received either sucrose or water both times), reductions in behavioural scores were noted in the sucrose group compared with the water group. Infants in the 25% sucrose group displayed a reduction in behavioural score (P = 0.001) when sucrose was given intra‐orally compared to via a nasogastric tube. Rushforth 1993 used a low concentration of sucrose (7.5%) and found no difference in median percentage crying time between the sucrose group and the group that received water. In Stevens 2005a, 66 preterm infants were randomised to (i) standard of care (positioning + swaddling), (ii) sterile water plus pacifier, or (iii) 24% sucrose plus pacifier two minutes prior to all painful procedures during the first 28 days of life. PIPP scores were recorded on day 7, 14, 21, and 28 following heel lance. There was a main effect of group (P = 0.03) with differences occurring between the sucrose plus pacifier group and standard care group at 60 s (P = 0.01). Mean PIPP scores were generally higher in the standard care group. In Stevens 2018, there was no difference between PIPP‐Revised (PIPP‐R) scores in infants randomised to receive 0.1 mL, 0.5 mL, and 1.0 mL of 24% sucrose. Storm 2002 reported less crying in infants in the groups that received sucrose (25%) or sucrose (25%) plus milk compared to the groups that received a lower concentration of sucrose (15%) or milk. Thakkar 2016 reported lower median PIPP scores in the sucrose combined with the NNS group compared to the sucrose only, NNS only, or no intervention groups. Yilmaz 2010 reported that the mean crying time in the sucrose group was lower than in the groups where the infant was sitting on the mother's lap, received mother's milk by syringe, or was given a pacifier.

    Effects of sucrose on long‐term neurodevelopmental outcomes

    None of the included studies reported on long‐term neurodevelopmental outcomes (assessed by a standardised and validated assessment tool, a child developmental specialist, or both) at 18 to 24 months or at any later age in childhood.

    Adverse effects

    Stevens 2013 reported seven studies that evaluated the adverse effects of sucrose compared to placebo (Codipietro 2008; Gibbins 2002; Guala 2001; Ramenghi 1996b; Stevens 1999; Stevens 2005a; Taddio 2008). Three of these studies observed minor immediate adverse effects in infants (Gibbins 2002; Stevens 2005a; Taddio 2008). Gibbins 2002 described minor adverse effects in six infants, none of which occurred in the sucrose + pacifier group. One neonate who received water + pacifier choked when the water was administered but stabilised within 10 s. Three infants randomised to the sucrose group and two infants randomised to the water + pacifier groups experienced oxygen desaturation when the study intervention (sucrose or water) was administered. Each neonate recovered spontaneously with no medical intervention required. Stevens 2005a reported that the frequency of adverse events related to repeated use of sucrose over the first 28 days of life was 'low' and all immediate adverse events resolved spontaneously. Taddio 2008 reported no differences between groups in blood glucose levels monitored during the study, or between the incidence of spitting up the sucrose solution.

    Tutag Lehr 2015 assessed adverse events including gagging, choking, and vomiting and noted that only one infant in the sucrose group experienced a mild episode of 'spitting up'. Thakkar 2016 reported on a total of five episodes of adverse effects in all four groups: one neonate each in the sucrose group, the NNS group and the sucrose + NNS group desaturated, with two neonates desaturated in the no intervention group. Three studies (Leng 2015; Marin Gabriel 2013; Simonse 2012), noted no adverse effects in any infant. Abbasoglu 2015 stated that no child developed any clinically visible changes on the skin, and that no adverse effects were observed in their comparison of laser acupuncture with oral sucrose.

    For the newly identified studies, adverse effects were reported in eight studies (Campbell‐Yeo 2019; Gao 2018; Kumari 2017; McGinnis 2016; Stevens 2018; Benoit 2021; Lago 2020; Velumula 2020). Four studies (Benoit 2021; Lago 2020; McGinnis 2016; Velumula 2020), observed no adverse effects in any infants. Gao 2018 reported three episodes of vomiting (one in the routine care group, one in the NNS group, and one in the sucrose + NNS group) and two infants who had abdominal distension (one in the sucrose + NNS group and one in the routine care group). In the 25% glucose group, Kumari 2017 observed one infant who developed coughing and another with vomiting which was present transiently and required no intervention. Stevens 2018 reported five adverse events, where three infants experienced gagging, one had a heart rate < 80 bpm, and another developed 80% oxygen saturation following sucrose administration. There were no differences in the proportion of adverse events between the intervention groups, and all resolved spontaneously. No differences were reported for adverse events (bradycardia, oxygen desaturation) between groups in the study by Campbell‐Yeo 2019. Researchers in more recent studies appear to be much more vigilant in observing and reporting adverse events. The proportion of minor adverse events remains very low with no major adverse events reported.

    Discussion

    Summary of main results

    Effectiveness of sucrose for pain associated with heel lance

    There were 13 newly identified studies added to this review, including term and preterm infants who received sucrose in doses ranging from 0.1 mL to 1 mL of 20% to 24% sucrose.

    Evidence from seven studies in 547 term and preterm infants suggests that sucrose (20‐30%, 0.1‐2 mL), with or without NNS, probably results in a reduction in PIPP scores compared to the control group at 30 seconds after lancing; at 60 seconds after heel lancing, sucrose probably results in a reduction in PIPP scores compared with water in two studies (164 infants) (summary of findings Table 1). Results of individual studies indicate that sucrose (24%) probably results in a reduction in NIPS scores immediately after heel lancing compared to infants receiving water (56 infants) (Tutag Lehr 2015) but not in DAN scores for sucrose and water groups (Mathai 2006).

    In a single study with 94 preterm infants (Kumari 2017), the evidence suggests that there is little to no difference in PIPP scores when comparing sucrose (24%) to glucose (25%) at 30 seconds and 60 seconds after heel lancing (summary of findings Table 4).

    Evidence from two combined studies in 148 term infants is very uncertain about the effect of 24% to 25% sucrose with or without NNS compared to breast milk on PIPP scores at 30 seconds after heel lancing. Similarly, the evidence is very uncertain about the effect of sucrose (24%) compared to breastfeeding in COMFORTneo scores after lancing in a single study involving 47 preterm infants (Simonse 2012) (summary of findings Table 5).

    When comparing sucrose and expressed breast milk in a single study with 94 preterm infants (Velumula 2020), the evidence suggests that sucrose (24%) may result in little to no difference in PIPP‐R scores compared to expressed breast milk during heel lancing and 30 seconds after heel lancing; however, at 60 seconds after heel lance, sucrose may result in slightly increased PIPP‐R scores compared to expressed breast milk (based on a single study of 94 preterm infants) (summary of findings Table 6).

    In terms of secondary outcomes (e.g. crying time, heart rate, oxygen saturation), the evidence is overall very uncertain about the effects of sucrose on individual behavioural and/or neurophysiological pain indicators.

    In the many studies that could not be included in the RevMan meta‐analyses, or reported on outcomes other than validated pain assessment scores, the evidence is overall very uncertain about the effects of sucrose on individual behavioural and/or neurophysiological pain indicators (see summary in the Results section).

    Long‐term neurodevelopmental outcomes

    Post‐discharge or long‐term neurodevelopmental outcomes were not assessed in any of the included studies on the effectiveness of sucrose for single heel lances.

    Adverse effects

    Adverse effects were evaluated in 22 studies; of these, 14 studies reported no adverse effects. For the remainder, most adverse events were minor and self‐resolved. There were no differences in adverse events between the sucrose groups and control groups. One study that measured blood glucose levels did not find any difference between the sucrose and the water group (Taddio 2008).

    Adverse effects following single event use of sucrose were not a documented issue in these studies.

    Overall completeness and applicability of evidence

    Although sucrose for pain from heel lance has been studied in 55 trials that included 6273 infants, the large variation in heel lancing procedures, dose of sucrose (concentration and volume), co‐interventions in the comparison groups, assessment tools used to assess effectiveness outcomes, and differences in the infant population (term or preterm born, postnatal age, severity of illness) resulted in a large number of RevMan analyses that included one or few trials. There were few infants included for each comparison and each outcome, which weakens our ability to draw firm conclusions. However, if the effectiveness of sucrose for a specific intervention was measured by a variety of assessment measures, and the results showed a similar reduction, this would strengthen the results and the conclusions.

    Quality of the evidence

    The certainty of the evidence varied from very low to moderate for the use of sucrose for analgesia for heel lance procedures in term and preterm neonates. Comparisons of the certainty of the evidence are reported in the various Summary of Findings tables. We excluded quasi‐randomised trials and trials reported in abstract form only. We included only a few studies for most comparisons and outcomes. For most outcomes, we included the results of a single study only; therefore, tests for heterogeneity were not applicable. The main reasons for our inability to include study results in RevMan analyses were that the results were not presented as means and SDs or were presented in graph form only.

    Potential biases in the review process

    We are not aware of any biases in the review process. Two authors (LS, JY) selected the trials from the literature searches and there was 100% agreement. Two authors (MB, LS) abstracted data and filled in predesigned forms for Risk of bias assessments and data abstraction, and one author (JY) checked the data abstraction. Any discrepancies were discussed and resolved by consensus. Three authors (JY, MCY, BS) are co‐authors on some included trials. For these trials, two authors (MB, LS) did the data abstraction and RoB assessments. One author (BS) is the developer of the PIPP and PIPP‐R measure and did not participate in any analyses of the quality of existing trials.

    Agreements and disagreements with other studies or reviews

    Previous reviews focusing on the analgesic effects of sucrose for different types of skin breaking procedures (Stevens 2001; Stevens 2004; Stevens 2010; Stevens 2013; Stevens 2016) reported inconsistency in effective sucrose dosage. Johnston 1997 and Stevens 1999 identified that very small volumes of 24% sucrose (0.1 mL) reduced procedural pain in infants. However, the meta‐analyses in Stevens 1997a showed 0.18 grams of sucrose were ineffective in reducing crying and did not differ from water. The evidence suggests that sucrose (20‐30%, 0.1‐2 mL), with or without NNS, probably results in a reduction in PIPP scores compared to the control group at 30 seconds after lancing. In these studies, there was a 0.5 to 2.0 point reduction in the PIPP score. Shah 2004 reported that clinicians and researchers consider a 20% reduction in pain to be the minimal clinically important difference, although other researchers suggest a 10% reduction may suffice (Powell 2001). Determining the level of clinical improvement is challenging in infants, given their inability to self‐report their pain and their reliance on observers to determine both pain intensity and the level of improvement that could make important contributions to treatment strategies.

    Stevens 2018 addressed the gap in knowledge identified in the 2016 sucrose review where the authors recommended additional research to identify the minimally effective dose of sucrose during a single painful procedure (Stevens 2016). No difference in mean pain intensity PIPP‐R scores was found between term and preterm infants who received 0.1 mL, 0.5 mL or 1.0 mL of 24% sucrose for a single heel‐lance procedure. The smallest amount of 24% sucrose (0.1 mL) was equally effective in reducing pain intensity in infants up to 60 seconds after the heel‐lance. The authors concluded that the smallest dose could be used effectively for procedural pain treatment, particularly heel‐lances.

    The greatest analgesic effect appears to occur when the full dose of sucrose is administered approximately two minutes before the painful stimulus. This interval is thought to coincide with the release of endogenous opioids (Blass 1994). However, Johnston 1999 reported increased analgesia when sucrose solution was repeatedly administered in small aliquots (i.e. 0.05 mL of 24% sucrose) at two‐minute intervals throughout the painful procedure. Future researchers need to further investigate the effectiveness of bolus versus small aliquot delivery of sucrose for single procedures. The peak effect appears to last about five minutes (Harrison 2020); therefore, the analgesic effect may wear off if procedures are prolonged. Because there is a wide variation in the duration of procedures commonly performed in infants, as well as in the extent of tissue damage inflicted by them (Laudiano‐Dray 2020), other strategies may be considered for prolonged and more invasive procedures. For example, lumbar punctures, endotracheal intubation, injections, and arterial punctures were considered of severe pain intensity (Laudiano‐Dray 2020) and may require a combination of pain‐relieving strategies, including pharmacological treatment. The infant's postnatal age may influence the effectiveness of sucrose (Taddio 2008).

    Generally, infants in this review were healthy term and preterm neonate infants and very few were under 27 weeks' GA at birth. Although the responses of preterm infants who were greater than 27 weeks GA at birth was generally consistent with that of the term infant, it is often more subtle, less sustained, and affected by the infant's behavioural state and severity of illness (Gibbins 2008). Given the paucity of knowledge in this age group, more research on preterm infants < 27 weeks' GA is warranted.

    The evidence was very uncertain about the effects of sucrose on secondary outcomes such as crying in term and preterm infants; however, the incidence of crying following painful stimuli is reported to be 50% less in preterm infants compared to term infants (Stevens 1994); therefore, crying alone may not be a valid or reliable indicator of pain in the preterm infant population and is precluded as an indicator in many validated infant pain measures, although it is often used as a unidimensional indicator of pain.

    Few researchers provided a definition or conceptualisation of pain as an outcome. In a recent revision of the IASP definition of pain (Raja 2020), authors acknowledge that all children including very preterm infants can and do experience pain. Having a clear definition of what pain entails in this vulnerable population provides a basis for measuring the quantity and quality of pain, and weighs the risks and benefits of targeted interventions (Stevens 2021). We need to make pain more visible, through optimising existing age‐appropriate pain measures, and understanding biological correlates. The use of psychometrically sound pain assessment tools available for neonates as primary outcomes in trials investigating the effectiveness and safety of pain relief interventions for acute pain should be prioritised. The results of a limited number of studies reporting pain intensity scores were combined and provided some evidence on the effectiveness of sucrose analgesia for heel‐lance procedures. However, a large portion of the studies considered behavioural outcomes, such as proportion, percentage or duration of time crying to be the most valid indicator of pain in neonates. Although research on infant crying has delineated certain crying characteristics, such as pitch, intensity, melody and harmonics, as being good indicators of pain, these were not assessed in the sucrose studies reviewed. Cry duration may give some indication of distress. However, cry duration does not necessarily confirm or deny that the infant is in pain. For unstable ventilated infants who often do not cry following painful procedures, any cry characteristic would be an inappropriate outcome. Attempts at cry or a silent cry in ventilated infants may be reasonable to consider. A multivariate approach looking at multiple indicators simultaneously or a reliable and validated composite pain score may be a more comprehensive approach.

    The majority of researchers provided little detail about the heel lance procedure in their studies (e.g. type of lancet used, number of attempts, number of squeezes, duration of the procedure). Therefore, it was impossible to determine if the painful stimuli were comparable in intensity, duration or frequency both within and between studies. The length of observation in terms of initial response (pain reactivity) and return to baseline (recovery) parameters (e.g. heart rate) of infants following procedures were not reported frequently.

    The delivery modality of sucrose (by syringe, dropper or dipping pacifier), where it was delivered and whether the full dose was delivered in a bolus or gradually dripped over a specified period, varied amongst studies. The pacifier promotes NNS and calming that may contribute to reducing pain‐elicited distress (Campos 1994). Blass 1994 suggested that sucking exerts a profound behavioural effect and induces feelings of calm. Other researchers found that NNS reduced heart rate and metabolic rate, promoted self‐soothing, and elevated the pain threshold. NNS has not been shown to affect cortisol response, vagal tone or oxygen saturation of blood (DiPietro 1994; Gunnar 1992). The calming effects are not sustained following cessation of the NNS alone. In contrast, the effects of NNS + sucrose administration, persist for several minutes beyond the cessation of contact. Results from this review indicate that the use of sucrose with NNS appears to have a synergistic effect with both single and repeated doses of sucrose. A systematic review on the efficacy and safety of oral sucrose with NNS for procedural pain (including heel lance) reported that sucrose + NNS might be superior in relieving mild pain in neonate infants (Li 2022).

    Codipietro 2008 concluded that breastfeeding was more effective than sucrose for reducing pain from heel lance in term infants. Shah 2012 recommended that breastfeeding, when available, should be used to reduce procedural pain in neonates who are exposed to single painful procedures; breast milk alone in small volumes was only as effective as water for the relief of procedural pain (much less so than sucrose), and its effectiveness for repeated painful procedures has not yet been established. Based on a recent systematic review, Benoit 2017a concluded that breastfeeding was as effective or more effective than sweet‐tasting solutions in full‐term infants, but this has not been demonstrated in preterm infants. Further research on direct comparisons between sucrose and parent‐driven interventions to minimise pain, such as breastfeeding and skin‐to‐skin contact, is warranted.

    Pillai Riddell 2015 in a systematic review of non‐pharmacological interventions (not including sucrose) found that the most established evidence for treating needle‐related procedures in preterm and term neonate infants and older infants was for NNS, swaddling, facilitated tucking, and rocking/holding. More recently, Harrison 2017 conducted a systematic review and cumulative meta‐analysis (CMA) to evaluate the effects of sweet solutions (sucrose or glucose) for neonatal procedural pain. From the 168 trials included in the review, results of 62 studies were eligible for inclusion in the CMA. Since the first published trials in the early 2000s, there was sufficient evidence to support the effectiveness of sweet solutions for procedural pain relief in neonates compared with placebo or no intervention.

    Slater 2010 demonstrated little to no differences in the nociceptive brain activity measured with neonatal EEG or magnitude or latency of the spinal nociceptive reflex withdrawal measured with EMG between infants given sucrose and those given sterile water; however, the PIPP scores were lower in the infants given sucrose. The authors suggested that oral sucrose does not affect activity in the neonatal brain or spinal cord and may not be an effective analgesic. The small sample size, moderate attrition rates and methods used to measure and analyse EEG and EMG recordings limit the generalisation of these findings; thus, further research is warranted.

    This systematic review evidence on sucrose has been integrated into evidence‐based sucrose consensus protocols and guidelines (AAP 2016; RNAO 2013). However, in an evaluation of infant pain guidelines (Lee 2014), recommendations were not sufficient, clear or consistent enough to guide clinical practice. Furthermore, in a study analysing pain practices during intensive care hospitalisation, pain‐relieving interventions were documented for 58.5% of procedures performed in neonates; sucrose (24%) was used for 34.2% of these procedures (Orovec 2019).

    There has been excellent progress in our understanding of the mechanisms underlying nociception and pain, but we still have serious gaps in our understanding of how pain is processed in the developing brain and optimal assessment and treatment approaches (Eccleston 2021; Fitzgerald 2015). There are important efforts being directed towards brain‐derived measures (e.g. EEG, near‐infrared spectroscopy) that will aid in distinguishing between noxious and non‐noxious events (Eccleston 2021). Methodological and pain treatment issues need to be addressed, and new evidence generated in light of the existing evidence. A comprehensive approach to validating various measures to evaluate pain in neonates and a standardised approach to measuring outcomes is critical, especially when evaluating pain‐relieving interventions that form the basis for clinical decision‐making (Bueno 2020). Although further studies are still required, neurophysiological approaches for pain assessment in infants appear to hold promise as indicators for supplementing existing measures (Benoit 2017b).

    Adverse effects following the short‐term use of sucrose with single painful events are currently reported as minimal, minor and self‐resolving. We reported the possible adverse effects in Table 1; Table 2; and Table 3. Adverse effects would be more likely to occur when multiple doses of sucrose are used. Stevens 2005a found no differences in incidence rates for necrotising enterocolitis between infants who received repeated doses of sucrose over 28 days of life compared to control groups. Johnston 2002 studied 107 preterm infants of less than 31 weeks' postmenstrual age who were randomised to receive either 1 mL of 24% sucrose or sterile water up to three times, two minutes apart, for all painful procedures over a seven‐day period. Higher frequency of sucrose doses was predictive of lower awareness, orientation (AO), and motor development and vigour (MDV) on the NAPI scale at 36 weeks, and lower (MDV) at 40 weeks. At two weeks' postnatal age, a higher number of doses of sucrose was predictive of higher Neuro‐Biological Risk Score (NBRS) scores. Proposed explanations were that: (a) low neurodevelopmental scores could be related to infants receiving sucrose during the one‐week study period only, and ongoing exposure to painful procedures might have resulted in heightened sensitivity to pain; or (b) the sample size was inadequate to identify other explanatory variables. Further analysis of these same data five years later revealed that 10 or fewer doses of sucrose over a 24‐hour period were unlikely to be related to poorer neurodevelopmental scores at term equivalence, although no follow‐up testing was conducted to determine if these differences were sustained (Johnston 2007). Banga 2016 reported that 93 infants randomised to either repeated doses of sucrose or water for painful procedures for seven consecutive days showed no differences in MDV or AO scores of the NAPI scale or adverse events. Stevens 2005a reported no differences between sucrose + pacifier, water + pacifier, or the standard care group on neurobiological risk status outcomes.

    The biochemical effects following sucrose administration are being explored. Asmerom 2013 indicated that a single dose of 24% sucrose (0.5‐2 mL, according to infant's weight) administered prior to heel‐lance resulted in increase of adenosine triphosphate (ATP) degradation and oxidative stress in preterm infants. In a subsequent study, Angeles 2015 reported that preterm infants who were intubated and/or receiving ⩾30% FiO2 displayed increased markers of ATP use following a single dose of oral sucrose given prior to heel lance. These results indicate the need to further investigate the short‐term effects of sucrose administration, on objective markers such as ATP degradation, oxidative stress, and cell injury.

    McPherson 2020 argues that there is still a limited understanding of the mechanism and efficacy of sucrose administration and therefore caution is required for repeated use in regard to long‐term neurodevelopmental outcomes, especially for preterm infants who are frequently exposed to repeated doses of sucrose in the early stages of brain development.

    The results of this current review indicate the need for further research on the long‐term effects of repeated doses of sucrose (AAP 2016; Stevens 2018), where total volume administered for procedural pain over time should be tracked, and the proportion of painful events with/without pain‐relieving intervention(s) and characteristics of the infant (e.g. GA, PNA, severity of illness) should be taken into account. Long‐term neurodevelopmental outcomes should be assessed using a standardised and validated assessment tool, a child developmental specialist, or both at 18 to 24 months or at any later age in childhood (AAP 2016).

    GRADE Working Group grades of evidence
    High certainty: we are very confident that the true effect lies close to that of the estimate of the effect.
    Moderate certainty: we are moderately confident in the effect estimate: the true effect is likely to be close to the estimate of the effect, but there is a possibility that it is substantially different.
    Low certainty: our confidence in the effect estimate is limited: the true effect may be substantially different from the estimate of the effect.
    Very low certainty: we have very little confidence in the effect estimate: the true effect is likely to be substantially different from the estimate of effect.

    Table 1. Characteristics of included studies assessing pain during heel lances

    Study

    Participants

    Procedure

    Interventions

    Outcomes

    Metrics used

    Results

    Funding sources and declaration of interest

    Abbasoglu 2015

    42 term newborns, undergoing heel lancing between postnatal days 3 and 8 as part of neonatal screening

    Heel lance

    • Laser acupuncture: 0.3 J of energy was applied to the Yintang point using a Laser PREMIO‐30 unit for 30 s

    • Sucrose: 0.5 mL 24% solution was given orally via syringe 2 min before heel lancing

    • NIPS

    • Crying time

    • Adverse events

    Mean and SD

    The NIPS score was lower in the sucrose group than in the laser group (3.66 ± 1.01 vs 4.52 ± 0.87; P = 0.006).

    The crying time was lower in the sucrose group than in the laser group (46.66 s ± 37.82 s vs 97.95 s ± 34.23 s; P = 0.000).

    No infant developed any clinically visible changes on the skin, and no side effects were observed in their comparison of laser acupuncture with oral sucrose.

    Data are included in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: not specified

    Declaration of interest: none declared

    Altun‐Köroğlu 2010

    75 full‐term infants

    Heel lance

    • 3 mL hindmilk (n = 25)

    • 3 mL 12.5% sucrose solution (n = 25)

    • 3 mL distilled water (n = 25)

    The volume of 3 mL was provided in 3 doses (1 mL prior to, 1 mL immediately before, and 1 mL after the procedure)

    • NFCS

    • Crying time

    • Duration of crying

    • HR

    Median and IQR

    Median crying time, duration of first cry and tachycardia, and time needed to return to baseline were longest in the distilled water group and shorter in the hindmilk group when compared to distilled water group (P = 0.022, P = 0.008, P = 0.009 and P = 0.038, respectively).

    No differences were observed between the hindmilk and sucrose group for all measures.

    Maximum HR in hindmilk group was lower than in distilled water group (184 beats/min vs 196 beats/min, P = 0.031).

    Reduction in average NFCS score. First‐minute NFCS score and fifth‐minute NFCS scores were lower in the hindmilk group compared to the distilled water group (P = 0.006, P = 0.017 and P = 0.021, respectively).

    No data were included in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: not specified

    Declaration of interest: not specified

    Asmerom 2013

    131 preterm infants ≤ 36.5 weeks' gestation who:

    • weighed ≥ 800 g;

    • had a central catheter in place; and

    • required a heel lance

    Heel lance

    • Sucrose (24%) with NNS (n = 44): 2 mL for neonates > 2 kg, 1.5 mL for neonates 1.5 kg to 2 kg, and 0.5 mL for neonates < 1.5 kg

    • Placebo + NNS (n = 45)

    • 42 infants received no heel lance, no sucrose, or placebo.

    • PIPP after 2 min

    • Plasma hypoxanthine

    • Uric acid

    • Xanthine

    • Allantoin

    • HR

    • SpO2

    Mean and lowest and highest values

    Oral sucrose given before a single heel lance decreased behavioural markers of pain.
    Sucrose increased markers of ATP use, as evidenced by increases over time in plasma hypoxanthine and uric acid concentrations.
    We received the PIPP scores as means and SD from Dr D Angeles, and the data were included in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: National Institutes of Health (RO1

    NR011209)

    Declaration of interest: none.

    Benoit 2021

    39 healthy full‐term (born ≥ 37 0/7 weeks' gestation)

    Heel lance

    • BF (n = 20)

    • 24% sucrose (0.24 mL) + NNS + containment in a blanket while in an infant cot 2 min prior to heel lance (n = 19)

    • Pain‐related potential was recorded on electroencephalogram

    • PIPP‐R at 30 s, 90 s, 120 s after heel lance

    • Physiologic recovery

    • Adverse events

    PIPP‐R scores: t‐tests were reported; no means or SD

    Infants in BF group had a smaller, but not different (F[1,15.9] = 0.58, P = 0.64, SE = 11.79), amplitude pain‐related potential (peak amplitude 0.29 μV) following heel lance compared to infants who received oral sucrose (peak amplitude 8.97 μV). Mean PIPP‐R scores were not different between groups following heel lance, however, but represented low pain across groups. Mean time in seconds to physiologic recovery was faster in BF group (M = 17.5, SD = 31.1) compared to oral sucrose group (M = 70.8, SD = 144.3).

    There were no adverse events or safety concerns in either group.

    Funding source: IWK Health Centre Category A Operating Grant and the Dalhousie University School of Nursing Research Fund

    Declaration of interest: none

    Blass 1997

    72 term infants, 22 h to 40 h old

    Heel lance

    2 mL of one of the following solutions:

    • water;

    • 12% sucrose;

    • protein mixture;

    • 7% lactose;

    • dilute fat (coconut and soy oil);

    • concentrated fat;

    • fat and lactose mixture RSF (water, protein, lactose, fat); and

    • milk

    n = 8 for all groups

    • Crying time (%) during blood collection and 1 min, 2 min and 3 min after heel lance

    • Mean percentage of crying time per minute at 1 min, 2 min and 3 min after heel lance (recovery period)

    • Mean proportions

    • Graphically reported

    Less crying time during blood collection in the sucrose group (47%) compared to the water group (92%, P = 0.015)

    No data could be used in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: Grant in Aid of Research MH

    51705 and by Research Scientist Award MH 00524 from the National Institutes of Mental Health

    Declaration of interest: none

    Blass 1999

    40 term newborn infants, 34 h to 55 h old

    Heel lance

    Prior to heel lance:

    • 2 mL 12% sucrose over 2 min via syringe (n = 10);

    • 2 mL water via syringe over 2 min (n = 10);

    • pacifier dipped every 30 s in 12% sucrose solution for 2 min (n = 10); and

    • pacifier dipped in water every 30 s for 2 min (n = 10)

    • Percentage of time crying 3 min after heel lance

    • Mean change in HR

    • Percentage of time grimacing

    • Mean percentage

    • Mean change (beats/min)

    • Graphically reported

    2 mL 12% (0.24 g) sucrose alone diminished cry duration from heel lance compared to water (8% vs 50%, P = 0.003) and water with pacifier (8% vs 35%, P = 0.002). Pacifier with 12% sucrose was more effective in reducing cry duration compared to water with pacifier (5% vs 35%, P = 0.001) or water alone (50%, P = 0.002).

    Mean HR increased from treatment to heel lance in infants receiving water alone (mean increase of 17 beats/min, P = 0.002) and water with pacifier (mean increase of 20 beats/min, P = 0.005). Mean increase in HR also increased for the 12% sucrose and pacifier group (mean difference of 7.4 beats/min, P = 0.05) but not for infants receiving 12% sucrose alone (mean difference of 5.9 beats/min, P = 0.142).

    12% sucrose reduced grimacing compared to water (P = 0.0003). 12% sucrose with pacifier reduced grimacing compared to water (P = 0.001) and pacifier alone (P = 0.04).

    No data could be used in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: Grant RO‐1

    MH51705‐04A1 and Research Scientist Award KO‐5,

    MH5 00524, from the National Institutes of Mental Health to EMB

    Declaration of interest: not specified

    Bucher 1995

    16 preterm infants, 27 weeks' to 34 weeks' GA, PNA approximately 42 d

    Heel lance

    • 2 mL 50% sucrose via syringe into the mouth 2 min before heel lance

    • 2 mL distilled water via syringe into the mouth 2 min before heel lance (n = 16, cross‐over design)

    • Percentage of time crying

    • Recovery time until crying stopped

    • Increase in HR

    • Recovery time for HR;

    • TcPO2 (maximum increase, measured in kPa; maximum decrease, in kPa; difference between baseline and 10 min after end of intervention ‐ kPa);

    • TcPCO2 (maximum decrease, in kPa; difference between baseline and 10 min after the end of intervention)

    • Recovery time for respirations

    • Cerebral near‐infrared spectroscopy (cerebral oxyhaemoglobin, deoxyhaemoglobin, total blood volume)

    Median, IQR

    Cry duration (percentage of total duration of intervention) was reduced in 2 mL 50% (1.0 g) sucrose group (71.5%) compared to control group (93.5%, P = 0.002).

    Median increase in HR (beats/min) after heel lance was reduced in the 2 mL 50% (1.0 g) sucrose group (35 beats/min) compared to water (51 beats/min) (P = 0.005).

    No differences between groups with respect to measures for TcPO2 (P = 0.05) and TcPCO2 (P = 0.21)

    There was no decrease in cerebral blood volume between sucrose and placebo groups.

    Data were not presented for effects of sucrose or water prior to cross over. No data could be used in RevMan analyses.

    Funding source: not specified

    Declaration of interest: not specified

    Chang 2020

    226 term infants

    Heel lance at > 24 h old when receiving the heel lance procedure as a newborn
    screen test

    • BF group (n = 45), initiated before heel lance and during heel lance procedure

    • Oral sucrose group (n = 42), 24% oral sucrose‐5 drops (0.33 mL) before heel lance and again during procedure if cues of pain, up to 15 drops (1 mL)

    • NNS group (n = 51), pacifier given before and during heel lance

    • SSC group (n = 38), newborns wore only diapers, positioned on mother's bare chest immediately before heel lance procedure

    • Control group (n = 50), newborns were placed in a crib in supine position and covered with blankets

    N‐PASS scores (duration of the heel lance was calculated from the point of lancet puncture until blood collection completion); N‐PASS scores were collected at these same procedure time points

    Mean, SD

    All intervention groups showed decreased pain levels compared with the control group (P < 0.01). The oral sucrose group experienced a superior analgesic effect compared to the SSC group (P < 0.01), but there was no difference when compared with the BF group
    (P > 0.05) or the NNS group (P > 0.05).

    All intervention groups had shortened crying times (P < 0.01) and reduced procedural duration (P < 0.01) compared with the control group.
